So we have two equations:

|a - c - α v| = α |v|,   (1)
|a - c - β v| = (1-β) |v|.   (2)

Since |v|>0, we can square and divide.

Let’s denote w = a - c. Then (1): |w - α v| = α |v|. Square: |w|^2 - 2α Re(w \overline{v}) + α^2 |v|^2 = α^2 |v|^2 => |w|^2 - 2α Re(w \overline{v}) = 0 => α = |w|^2 / (2 Re(w \overline{v})). Provided Re(w \overline{v}) > 0. Since α>0, we need Re(w \overline{v})>0.

Similarly, (2): |w - β v| = (1-β) |v|. Square: |w|^2 - 2β Re(w \overline{v}) + β^2 |v|^2 = (1-β)^2 |v|^2 = (1 - 2β + β^2) |v|^2 => |w|^2 - 2β Re(w \overline{v}) + β^2 |v|^2 = |v|^2 - 2β |v|^2 + β^2 |v|^2 => |w|^2 - 2β Re(w \overline{v}) = |v|^2 - 2β |v|^2 => |w|^2 - 2β Re(w \overline{v}) = |v|^2 (1 - 2β) => bring terms: |w|^2 - 2β Re(w \overline{v}) - |v|^2 + 2β |v|^2 = 0 => (|w|^2 - |v|^2) - 2β (Re(w \overline{v}) - |v|^2) = 0 => β = (|w|^2 - |v|^2) / (2 (Re(w \overline{v}) - |v|^2)). Provided denominator nonzero.

Set P=0. Let the unit circle (|z|=1) be the circumcircle of ACD. So A = a, C = c, D = d with |a|=|c|=|d|=1. Also, we have that M and N lie on line CD. So B = reflection of a over line through c and d. In complex numbers, reflection across a line is given by: if line has direction vector v, then the reflection of point z across the line through point p with direction v is: z' = p + (v/ \overline{v}) \overline{(z-p)}. But we can compute using that the line is the set of points of the form c + t(d-c). The reflection formula: Let u = (d-c)/|d-c| be a unit vector along line. Then reflection across line through point p (e.g., c) with direction u is: z' = p + u^2 \overline{(z-p)}? For line through point p, we translate: z' = p + u^2 \overline{(z-p)}. So B = c + u^2 \overline{(a-c)} where u = (d-c)/|d-c|. So B = c + ((d-c)^2/|d-c|^2) \overline{(a-c)}.

Compute |a-m|^2 - |c-m|^2 = 0. Since m = c + t(d-c), we have c-m = -t(d-c). So |c-m|^2 = t^2 |d-c|^2. And a-m = a - c - t(d-c) = w - t v, where w = a-c, v = d-c. Then condition: |w - t v|^2 = t^2 |v|^2 => |w|^2 - 2t Re(w \overline{v}) + t^2 |v|^2 = t^2 |v|^2 => |w|^2 - 2t Re(w \overline{v}) = 0 => t = |w|^2 / (2 Re(w \overline{v})). Similarly, n = c + s v, with s such that |a-n| = |d-n|. But careful: The condition for N is that N is circumcenter of Γ through A and D, so |a-n| = |d-n|. Write n = c + s v. Now, we need to compute E and F. E is second intersection of line OA (through origin and a) with Ω. Since Ω is circle with center M and radius r = |c-m|. Expand: |λ a|^2 - 2λ Re(a \overline{m}) + |m|^2 = |a|^2 - 2 Re(a \overline{m}) + |m|^2 => λ^2 |a|^2 - 2λ Re(a \overline{m}) = |a|^2 - 2 Re(a \overline{m}) => λ^2 - 2λ Re(a \overline{m})/|a|^2 = 1 - 2 Re(a \overline{m})/|a|^2. So m = e^{iθ} - i * (4 sin^2((α-θ)/2))/(2 (sinθ - sinα)) = e^{iθ} - i * (2 sin^2((α-θ)/2))/(sinθ - sinα). Now, sinθ - sinα = 2 cos((α+θ)/2) sin((θ-α)/2). And sin^2((α-θ)/2) = sin^2((θ-α)/2). So factor: 2 sin^2((θ-α)/2)/(2 cos((α+θ)/2) sin((θ-α)/2)) = sin((θ-α)/2)/cos((α+θ)/2) = tan((θ-α)/2) *? Actually, sin((θ-α)/2)/cos((α+θ)/2) =? So m = e^{iθ} - i * ( sin((θ-α)/2)/cos((α+θ)/2) ). Let’s denote δ = (θ-α)/2, and σ = (α+θ)/2. Then m = e^{iθ} - i * ( sinδ / cosσ ). But e^{iθ} = cosθ + i sinθ. So m = cosθ + i sinθ - i ( sinδ / cosσ ). So y_m = sinθ - sinδ / cosσ. So m = (cosθ, sinθ - sinδ/cosσ). This is a real coordinate expression.

Now, similarly, we need n = c + s v, with s = (|w|^2 - |v|^2)/(2(Re(w \overline{v}) - |v|^2)). But we can compute directly using formula for n from intersection of perpendicular bisector of AD with x=cosθ. Since D = e^{-iθ} = cosθ - i sinθ, we can compute similarly.

Alternatively, using complex: For N, we have condition |a-n| = |d-n|. Write n = c + s v. Then d-n = v - s v = (1-s)v. So |d-n| = |1-s||v|. And |a-n| = |w - s v|. So condition: |w - s v|^2 = (1-s)^2 |v|^2. This gave s = (|w|^2 - |v|^2)/(2(Re(w \overline{v}) - |v|^2)). But we can also compute n as intersection of x=cosθ with perpendicular bisector of AD. Let’s compute y_n.

Let Δx' = cosα - cosθ, Δy' = sinα - (-sinθ) = sinα + sinθ. Then midpoint of AD: ((cosθ+cosα)/2, (-sinθ+sinα)/2) = ((cosθ+cosα)/2, (sinα-sinθ)/2). Perpendicular bisector equation: (x - midpoint_x)*Δx' + (y - midpoint_y)*Δy' = 0. Plug x=cosθ.

Compute cosθ - (cosθ+cosα)/2 = (cosθ - cosα)/2 = -Δx'/2. So first term: (-Δx'/2)*Δx' = -Δx'^2/2.

Then (y - (sinα-sinθ)/2)*Δy' + (-Δx'^2/2) = 0 => (y - (sinα-sinθ)/2)*Δy' = Δx'^2/2 => y = (sinα-sinθ)/2 + Δx'^2/(2Δy'). So y_n = (sinα-sinθ)/2 + (cosα-cosθ)^2/(2(sinα+sinθ)).

Now, Δx' = cosα - cosθ, Δy' = sinα + sinθ.

Simplify using trig identities: cosα - cosθ = -2 sin((α+θ)/2) sin((α-θ)/2) = 2 sin((α+θ)/2) sin((θ-α)/2) (since sin((α-θ)/2) = - sin((θ-α)/2)). So Δx'^2 = 4 sin^2((α+θ)/2) sin^2((θ-α)/2). And sinα+sinθ = 2 sin((α+θ)/2) cos((α-θ)/2). So Δx'^2/(Δy') = (4 sin^2((α+θ)/2) sin^2((θ-α)/2)) / (2 sin((α+θ)/2) cos((α-θ)/2)) = 2 sin((α+θ)/2) sin^2((θ-α)/2)/cos((α-θ)/2). But note that cos((α-θ)/2) = cos((θ-α)/2) (since cos is even). So = 2 sin((α+θ)/2) sin^2((θ-α)/2)/cos((θ-α)/2) = 2 sin((α+θ)/2) sin((θ-α)/2) tan((θ-α)/2). So then y_n = (sinα-sinθ)/2 + (1/(2)) * (Δx'^2/Δy') = (sinα-sinθ)/2 + (1/2)* [2 sin((α+θ)/2) sin((θ-α)/2) tan((θ-α)/2)] = (sinα-sinθ)/2 + sin((α+θ)/2) sin((θ-α)/2) tan((θ-α)/2). Now, sinα-sinθ = 2 cos((α+θ)/2) sin((α-θ)/2) = -2 cos((α+θ)/2) sin((θ-α)/2). So (sinα-sinθ)/2 = - cos((α+θ)/2) sin((θ-α)/2). So y_n = - cos((α+θ)/2) sin((θ-α)/2) + sin((α+θ)/2) sin((θ-α)/2) tan((θ-α)/2) = sin((θ-α)/2)[ -cos((α+θ)/2) + sin((α+θ)/2) tan((θ-α)/2) ].

Now, sin((α+θ)/2) tan((θ-α)/2) = sin((α+θ)/2) * sin((θ-α)/2)/cos((θ-α)/2) = sin((α+θ)/2) sin((θ-α)/2)/cos((θ-α)/2). So y_n = sin((θ-α)/2)[ -cosσ + sinσ * sinδ/cosδ ] where σ = (α+θ)/2, δ = (θ-α)/2. But sinσ sinδ/cosδ = sinσ tanδ. So y_n = sinδ[ -cosσ + sinσ tanδ ] = - sinδ cosσ + sinδ sinσ tanδ = - sinδ cosσ + sinδ sinσ (sinδ/cosδ) = - sinδ cosσ + sinσ sin^2δ/cosδ.

Let's reconsider the problem with a synthetic approach.

Given circles Ω (center M, radius r) and Γ (center N, radius R), with r<R, intersecting at A and B. Line MN meets Ω at C (on the side of M opposite N) and Γ at D (on the side of N opposite M). So M is between C and N, N between M and D. So C, M, N, D collinear in that order.

Define P = circumcenter of triangle ACD. So P is the intersection of perpendicular bisectors of AC, AD, and CD. Since M is the circumcenter of ABC, we have M lies on the perpendicular bisector of AC. Similarly, N lies on the perpendicular bisector of AD. And P lies on both perpendicular bisectors of AC and AD. Therefore, PM is the perpendicular bisector of AC? Actually, P and M both lie on the perpendicular bisector of AC, so line PM is the perpendicular bisector of AC. Similarly, line PN is the perpendicular bisector of AD. So PM ⟂ AC and PN ⟂ AD.

Thus, triangle PMN has the property that the lines PM and PN are perpendicular to AC and AD respectively.

Now, consider the circumcircle of triangle ACD with center P. Points A, C, D lie on this circle.

We have AP meets Ω again at E and Γ again at F. So E and F are on Ω and Γ respectively, and also on line AP.

We also have H is orthocenter of triangle PMN. So H is the intersection of the altitudes from P, M, N.

Since M and N lie on line MN, the altitude from P is the line through P perpendicular to MN. The altitude from M is the line through M perpendicular to PN. The altitude from N is the line through N perpendicular to PM.

Now, note that PM ⟂ AC and PN ⟂ AD. So the altitude from M is perpendicular to PN, but PN is perpendicular to AD, so the altitude from M is parallel to AD? Because if line ℓ is perpendicular to PN, and PN ⟂ AD, then ℓ ∥ AD. Indeed, two lines perpendicular to the same line are parallel. So the altitude from M (through M) is perpendicular to PN, and since PN ⟂ AD, we have altitude from M ∥ AD. Similarly, altitude from N (through N) is perpendicular to PM, and since PM ⟂ AC, we have altitude from N ∥ AC. And altitude from P is perpendicular to MN.

Thus, we have:

- Line through M parallel to AD (since altitude from M ∥ AD) is the altitude from M, so it passes through H.
- Line through N parallel to AC (altitude from N) is the altitude from N, so it passes through H.
- Line through P perpendicular to MN is the altitude from P, so it passes through H.

So H is the intersection of:
- line through M parallel to AD,
- line through N parallel to AC,
- line through P perpendicular to MN.

Thus, we have a nice description: H is the point such that MH ∥ AD, NH ∥ AC, and PH ⟂ MN. (Check: Since altitude from M is perpendicular to PN, and PN ⟂ AD, so altitude from M ∥ AD. Yes.)

Now, we want to prove that the line through H parallel to AP is tangent to circumcircle of triangle BEF.

Since AP is a line, the line through H parallel to AP is just a line in the direction of AP. So L = line through H with direction AP.

Now, compute y_m:

y_m = sinθ - sinδ / cosσ = (sinσ cosδ + cosσ sinδ) - (sinδ)/cosσ.

So y_m = sinσ cosδ + cosσ sinδ - (sinδ)/cosσ.

Now, compute y_n using the formula:

y_n = (sinα - sinθ)/2 + (cosα - cosθ)^2/(2(sinα+sinθ)).

Compute sinα - sinθ = (sinσ cosδ - cosσ sinδ) - (sinσ cosδ + cosσ sinδ) = -2 cosσ sinδ.

So (sinα - sinθ)/2 = - cosσ sinδ.

Now, cosα - cosθ = (cosσ cosδ + sinσ sinδ) - (cosσ cosδ - sinσ sinδ) = 2 sinσ sinδ.

So (cosα - cosθ)^2 = 4 sin^2σ sin^2δ.

Now, sinα + sinθ = (sinσ cosδ - cosσ sinδ) + (sinσ cosδ + cosσ sinδ) = 2 sinσ cosδ.

Thus, (cosα-cosθ)^2/(2(sinα+sinθ)) = (4 sin^2σ sin^2δ)/(2 * 2 sinσ cosδ) = (4 sin^2σ sin^2δ)/(4 sinσ cosδ) = (sinσ sin^2δ)/cosδ = sinσ sinδ tanδ.

So y_n = - cosσ sinδ + sinσ sinδ tanδ = sinδ( -cosσ + sinσ tanδ ).

Thus, y_n = sinδ ( sinσ tanδ - cosσ ) = sinδ ( sinσ (sinδ/cosδ) - cosσ ) = sinδ ( (sinσ sinδ)/cosδ - cosσ ).

Now, note that y_m = sinσ cosδ + cosσ sinδ - sinδ/cosσ = sinσ cosδ + sinδ (cosσ - 1/cosσ). So y_m = sinσ cosδ + sinδ (cosσ - 1/cosσ). That is also = sinσ cosδ + sinδ ( (cos^2σ - 1)/cosσ ) = sinσ cosδ - sinδ (sin^2σ)/cosσ = sinσ cosδ - sinδ sin^2σ / cosσ.

So y_m = sinσ cosδ - (sinδ sin^2σ)/cosσ.

Similarly, y_n = sinδ ( sinσ tanδ - cosσ ) = sinδ ( sinσ (sinδ/cosδ) - cosσ ) = (sinδ sinσ sinδ)/cosδ - sinδ cosσ = sinσ sin^2δ / cosδ - sinδ cosσ.

Thus, y_n = sinσ sin^2δ / cosδ - sinδ cosσ.

So we have:

y_m = sinσ cosδ - (sin^2σ sinδ)/cosσ
y_n = (sinσ sin^2δ)/cosδ - sinδ cosσ

Now, compute y_m y_n. This will be used in H.

We also need e and f:

e = 2cosα cosθ + 2 sinα y_m - 1
f = 2cosα cosθ + 2 sinα y_n - 1

We can compute cosα cosθ and sinα.

cosα cosθ = cos(σ-δ) cos(σ+δ) = (cosσ cosδ + sinσ sinδ)(cosσ cosδ - sinσ sinδ) = (cosσ cosδ)^2 - (sinσ sinδ)^2 = cos^2σ cos^2δ - sin^2σ sin^2δ.

sinα = sin(σ-δ) = sinσ cosδ - cosσ sinδ.

Now, compute 2cosα cosθ = 2(cos^2σ cos^2δ - sin^2σ sin^2δ).

Then 2 sinα y_m = 2(sinσ cosδ - cosσ sinδ) * (sinσ cosδ - (sin^2σ sinδ)/cosσ).

Let's compute y_m in a more factorable form: y_m = sinσ cosδ - (sin^2σ sinδ)/cosσ = sinσ cosδ - sin^2σ sinδ / cosσ = sinσ (cosδ - (sinσ sinδ)/cosσ). But we have cosδ cosσ - sinσ sinδ = cos(σ+δ). So indeed, y_m = sinσ cos(σ+δ)/cosσ? Wait, sinσ cos(σ+δ) = sinσ (cosσ cosδ - sinσ sinδ) = sinσ cosσ cosδ - sin^2σ sinδ. Because sinσ cos(σ+δ) = sinσ cosσ cosδ - sin^2σ sinδ, dividing by cosσ gives sinσ cosδ - (sin^2σ sinδ)/cosσ. Yes, so y_m = (sinσ cos(σ+δ))/cosσ.

Similarly, y_n = sinσ sin^2δ / cosδ - sinδ cosσ = sinδ ( sinσ sinδ / cosδ - cosσ ) = sinδ ( tanδ sinσ - cosσ ). Also, note that sinσ sinδ / cosδ = sinσ tanδ. So y_n = sinδ ( sinσ tanδ - cosσ ). But can we write y_n as something like (sinδ cos(σ+δ))/cosδ? Let's compute cos(σ+δ) = cosσ cosδ - sinσ sinδ. Then sinδ cos(σ+δ) = sinδ cosσ cosδ - sinδ sinσ sinδ = sinδ cosσ cosδ - sinσ sin^2δ. Actually, y_n = sinσ sin^2δ / cosδ - sinδ cosσ = sinδ ( sinσ tanδ - cosσ ) = - sinδ (cosσ - sinσ tanδ). So y_n = - (sinδ cos(σ+δ))/cosδ. Because sinδ cos(σ+δ)/cosδ = sinδ (cosσ cosδ - sinσ sinδ)/cosδ = sinδ cosσ - sinσ sin^2δ/cosδ = sinδ cosσ - sinσ tanδ sinδ = sinδ (cosσ - sinσ tanδ). So y_n = - (sinδ cos(σ+δ))/cosδ.

y_m = (sinσ cos(σ+δ))/cosσ
y_n = - (sinδ cos(σ+δ))/cosδ

That is nice! Because cos(σ+δ) = cosθ. So cosθ = cos(σ+δ). So we have:

y_m = (sinσ cosθ)/cosσ
y_n = - (sinδ cosθ)/cosδ

Now, note that sinσ and sinδ are positive if σ,δ in (0,π/2). We'll assume generic.

AP is also x-axis because A is on x-axis and P is origin. So they coincide. So in this coordinate system, AP and AB are the same line. Let's attempt a synthetic approach.

Given circles Ω (center M) and Γ (center N) intersect at A and B. Line MN meets Ω at C and Γ at D, with order C-M-N-D. So M is between C and N, N between M and D.

Let P be circumcenter of triangle ACD. So P is the intersection of perpendicular bisectors of AC and AD. Since M is the circumcenter of triangle ABC, we have M lies on the perpendicular bisector of AC. Similarly, N lies on the perpendicular bisector of AD. Therefore, lines PM and PN are the perpendicular bisectors of AC and AD respectively. So PM ⟂ AC, PN ⟂ AD.

Now, consider the circumcircle of triangle ACD with center P. Its tangent at A is perpendicular to PA. So the tangent at A is perpendicular to line AP.

Now, let’s denote the line through H parallel to AP. Since AP is a line, we want to show that this line is tangent to circumcircle of BEF. Perhaps we can show that H lies on the radical axis of something, and the line is the polar of a point.

Another idea: Use the fact that the orthocenter H of triangle PMN satisfies that PH is perpendicular to MN, and MH is parallel to AD, NH is parallel to AC. So H is the point such that quadrilateral A? Might be that H is the center of a spiral similarity sending AC to AD? Not.

Given that we have a horizontal tangent line (since we set AP horizontal), we need to show that the horizontal line through H is tangent to circle (BEF). That is equivalent to showing that the circle (BEF) has a horizontal tangent at some point, and that point is such that the tangent line is y = y_H. So we need to find the y-coordinate of the center of (BEF) and its radius, and then check that the distance from the center to the line y = y_H equals the radius.

Thus, we need to compute the circumcenter O of triangle BEF. Since E and F are on x-axis, we can use coordinate geometry with A=(1,0). Let's do that.

Set:

P = (0,0)
A = (1,0)
C = (cosφ, sinφ)
D = (cosψ, sinψ)

We will assume that φ and ψ are such that the circles are properly defined. Not necessary for algebra.

First, compute M: intersection of line CD with perpendicular bisector of AC.

Let’s compute coordinates.

Let’s denote:

c = (c_x, c_y) = (cosφ, sinφ)
d = (d_x, d_y) = (cosψ, sinψ)

Line CD param: X = c + t(d-c) = (c_x + t(d_x-c_x), c_y + t(d_y-c_y))

Now, perpendicular bisector of AC:

A=(1,0), C=(c_x,c_y). Midpoint: ((1+c_x)/2, c_y/2). Direction of AC: (c_x-1, c_y). A perpendicular vector: (c_y, 1-c_x) (since dot: (c_x-1)*c_y + c_y*(1-c_x)=0). So the line: Y = ((1+c_x)/2, c_y/2) + s (c_y, 1-c_x).

Set c + t(d-c) = ((1+c_x)/2, c_y/2) + s (c_y, 1-c_x).

This gives two equations:

c_x + t(d_x-c_x) = (1+c_x)/2 + s c_y   ...(1)
c_y + t(d_y-c_y) = c_y/2 + s (1-c_x)   ...(2)

We can solve for t and s.

From (2): c_y + t(d_y-c_y) = c_y/2 + s(1-c_x) => (c_y/2) + t(d_y-c_y) = s(1-c_x) => s = (c_y/2 + t(d_y-c_y))/(1-c_x), provided 1-c_x ≠ 0 (i.e., φ not 0). If φ=0, then C=A, degenerate. So we assume φ≠0, and similarly ψ≠0.

From (1): c_x + t(d_x-c_x) = (1+c_x)/2 + s c_y => substitute s.

Let’s compute:

c_x + t(d_x-c_x) = (1+c_x)/2 + c_y * (c_y/2 + t(d_y-c_y))/(1-c_x)

Multiply both sides by (1-c_x):

(1-c_x)(c_x + t(d_x-c_x)) = (1-c_x)(1+c_x)/2 + c_y (c_y/2 + t(d_y-c_y))

Compute left: (1-c_x)c_x + t(1-c_x)(d_x-c_x) = c_x - c_x^2 + t(1-c_x)(d_x-c_x)

Right: (1-c_x)(1+c_x)/2 = (1-c_x^2)/2 + c_y^2/2 + t c_y (d_y-c_y)

Thus, we have:

c_x - c_x^2 + t(1-c_x)(d_x-c_x) = (1-c_x^2)/2 + c_y^2/2 + t c_y (d_y-c_y)

But c_x^2 + c_y^2 = 1. So 1-c_x^2 = c_y^2. So (1-c_x^2)/2 = c_y^2/2. Then the right side becomes c_y^2/2 + c_y^2/2 + t c_y (d_y-c_y) = c_y^2 + t c_y (d_y-c_y). So the equation simplifies to:

c_x - c_x^2 + t(1-c_x)(d_x-c_x) = c_y^2 + t c_y (d_y-c_y)

But c_x - c_x^2 = c_x(1-c_x). And c_y^2 = 1 - c_x^2 = (1-c_x)(1+c_x). So c_x(1-c_x) + t(1-c_x)(d_x-c_x) = (1-c_x)(1+c_x) + t c_y (d_y-c_y). Factor (1-c_x) from left: (1-c_x)[c_x + t(d_x-c_x)] = (1-c_x)(1+c_x) + t c_y (d_y-c_y). Since 1-c_x ≠ 0, divide both sides by (1-c_x):

c_x + t(d_x-c_x) = 1+c_x + t c_y (d_y-c_y)/(1-c_x). So:

t(d_x-c_x) = 1 + t c_y (d_y-c_y)/(1-c_x). So:

t(d_x-c_x) - t c_y (d_y-c_y)/(1-c_x) = 1 => t[ (d_x-c_x) - c_y (d_y-c_y)/(1-c_x) ] = 1. So t = 1 / [ (d_x-c_x) - c_y (d_y-c_y)/(1-c_x) ].

Now, that is a more manageable statement. Because we have explicit coordinates, and we need to show that the circle through B, E, F is tangent to the x-axis.

Let's compute coordinates explicitly in terms of σ and δ. We have:

cosθ = cos(σ+δ)

A = (cα, sα)
E = (e cα, e sα)
F = (f cα, f sα)
B = (2cθ - cα, sα)

We need to show that the circle through B, E, F is tangent to x-axis (y=0). That means that the x-axis is a tangent to that circle. Since the circle passes through points E and F which are not on x-axis (unless sα=0, which would make A on x-axis, leading to degenerate case), the circle will intersect x-axis at some points, but tangency means it touches at exactly one point. Equivalent to the power of the point of tangency? Actually, the condition that a line L is tangent to a circle is that the distance from the center to L equals the radius. So we need to find the circumcenter O of triangle BEF and check that its y-coordinate equals the radius (since line is y=0, distance = |y_O|). So we need to show that y_O = R, i.e., the center lies at a distance equal to radius from x-axis.

Alternatively, we can compute the equation of the circle in terms of coordinates and show that the discriminant of the intersection with y=0 is zero (i.e., the quadratic in x has a double root). Since the circle passes through points E and F, which are not on x-axis, but we can compute the circle's equation and then plug y=0. That might be easier because we have explicit coordinates.

Let's attempt to compute the circle (BEF) using coordinates.

Let’s denote:

U = (cα, sα)  (A)
E = eU
F = fU

B = (2cθ - cα, sα) = (2cθ - cα, sα)

Now, note that B is a point with the same y-coordinate as A (sα). So B lies on the horizontal line y = sα.

We want to find the circle through B, E, F. Since E and F lie on line through origin with direction U, we can use the concept of the circle through B and two points on a line. The circle's center O lies on the perpendicular bisector of EF, which is the line through the midpoint of EF perpendicular to U. Let's find that line.

Midpoint of EF: ( (e+f)/2 ) U. Let’s denote M_EF = ((e+f)/2) U. The perpendicular bisector of EF is the set of points X such that (X - M_EF) · U = 0 (since U is direction of EF). So equation: X · U = (e+f)/2 (since M_EF · U = (e+f)/2 |U|^2 = (e+f)/2). So the line is: (x,y) · (cα, sα) = (e+f)/2. That is a linear equation.

Thus, O satisfies: O · U = (e+f)/2.

Also, O is equidistant from B and E: |O - B|^2 = |O - E|^2. Since E = eU, we have:

|O - B|^2 = |O - eU|^2 => (O - B)·(O - B) = (O - eU)·(O - eU) => O·O - 2O·B + B·B = O·O - 2e O·U + e^2 U·U => -2O·B + B·B = -2e O·U + e^2 (since U·U=1). So:

2O·B - 2e O·U = B·B - e^2 => O·(2B - 2eU) = B·B - e^2 => O·(B - eU) = (B·B - e^2)/2.

Similarly, from |O - B|^2 = |O - fU|^2 gives O·(B - fU) = (B·B - f^2)/2.

Thus, O satisfies two linear equations: O·U = (e+f)/2, and O·(B - eU) = (B·B - e^2)/2. We can solve for O.

Write O = (x,y). Then we have:

x cα + y sα = (e+f)/2   (1)
x (B_x - e cα) + y (sα - e sα) = (B·B - e^2)/2   (2) since B_y = sα.

But B_x = 2cθ - cα, so B - eU = (2cθ - cα - e cα, sα - e sα) = (2cθ - (1+e)cα, (1-e)sα). So equation (2):

x (2cθ - (1+e)cα) + y (1-e)sα = (B·B - e^2)/2.

Now, we also have the analogous equation with f, but we can just use the two equations to solve for O. However, we only need to show that the x-axis is tangent to the circle. That is equivalent to the line y=0 being tangent, which means that the distance from O to y=0 equals the radius. Since the radius is R = |O - B| = |O - E|, we need to show that |y_O| = R. That is, the y-coordinate of O equals the distance from O to B (or E). That is a strong condition.

Alternatively, we can compute the circle's equation directly. Since E and F are on line through origin, we can find the circle by using the fact that the equation of a circle through points on a line can be expressed as (x^2+y^2) + Ax + By + C = 0. For points E = eU, we have e^2 + A e cα + B e sα + C = 0. For F: f^2 + A f cα + B f sα + C = 0. For B: B·B + A B_x + B B_y + C = 0. We can solve for A, B, C. Then the condition that y=0 is tangent means that the intersection of the circle with y=0 has exactly one point, i.e., the quadratic in x from plugging y=0 has discriminant zero. That quadratic: x^2 + A x + C = 0 (since y=0 gives x^2 + A x + C = 0). So discriminant Δ = A^2 - 4C = 0.

Thus, we need to compute A and C from the three points and then verify that A^2 = 4C.

Given our parametric expressions, we can try to compute A and C. Since we have many parameters, we can attempt to derive relationships.

Let's denote:

U = (cα, sα) with cα^2 + sα^2 = 1.
E = eU, F = fU.
B = (2cθ - cα, sα).

We need to find circle equation: X·X + A x + B y + C = 0 (where X=(x,y)). This is the general circle (not necessarily centered at origin). For points on the circle:

For E: e^2 + A e cα + B e sα + C = 0.   (E)
For F: f^2 + A f cα + B f sα + C = 0.   (F)
For B: B·B + A B_x + B sα + C = 0.   (B)

We can treat these as linear equations in A, B, C. Subtract (E) from (F): (f^2 - e^2) + A (f - e) cα + B (f - e) sα = 0 => (f - e)(f + e) + A (f - e) cα + B (f - e) sα = 0 => if f≠e, then f+e + A cα + B sα = 0 => A cα + B sα = -(e+f). So that gives one equation: A cα + B sα = -(e+f). (Equation 1)

Now, from (E): e^2 + A e cα + B e sα + C = 0 => e^2 + e (A cα + B sα) + C = 0 => e^2 + e (-(e+f)) + C = 0 => e^2 - e^2 - e f + C = 0 => C = e f. So we get C = e f.

Thus, the circle equation is: x^2 + y^2 + A x + B y + e f = 0, with A cα + B sα = -(e+f). (Equation 1)

Now, we also have the condition from B: B·B + A B_x + B sα + e f = 0 => B·B + A B_x + B sα = -e f.   (Equation 2)

So we have two equations (1) and (2) in A and B, and we can solve for A and B. Then the condition that y=0 is tangent is that the quadratic in x from setting y=0: x^2 + A x + e f = 0 has discriminant zero: A^2 - 4 e f = 0. So we need to prove that A^2 = 4 e f.

Thus, we need to compute A and B from (1) and (2) and then verify that A^2 = 4 e f.

Now, we have expressions for e, f, cα, sα, B_x, and B·B. Let's compute these in terms of σ and δ.

We have:

cα = cos(σ-δ) = cσ cδ + sσ sδ
sα = sin(σ-δ) = sσ cδ - cσ sδ

cθ = cos(σ+δ) = cσ cδ - sσ sδ
sθ = sin(σ+δ) = sσ cδ + cσ sδ

e = 2 cθ cδ / cσ - 1
f = 2 cθ cσ / cδ - 1

Now, we also need B·B = (2cθ - cα)^2 + sα^2 = 4cθ^2 -4cθ cα + cα^2 + sα^2 = 4cθ^2 -4cθ cα + 1 (since cα^2+sα^2=1). So B·B = 4cθ^2 -4cθ cα + 1.

Also, B_x = 2cθ - cα.

Now, we need to solve for A and B from:

(1) A cα + B sα = -(e+f)
(2) A B_x + B sα = -e f - (B·B? Actually, from (2): B·B + A B_x + B sα + e f = 0 => A B_x + B sα = - (B·B + e f). Wait, careful: (2) is B·B + A B_x + B sα + e f = 0 => A B_x + B sα = - (B·B + e f). So equation (2): A B_x + B sα = - (B·B + e f).

Thus, we have a linear system for A and B:

A cα + B sα = -(e+f)
A B_x + B sα = - (B·B + e f)

We can solve for A and B. Since cα and sα are not both zero, we can solve. Write the system as:

[ cα   sα ] [A] = [ - (e+f) ]
[ B_x  sα ] [B]   [ - (B·B + e f) ]

We can solve by Cramer's rule.

Let D = determinant of coefficient matrix = cα * sα - sα * B_x = sα (cα - B_x). But B_x = 2cθ - cα. So cα - B_x = cα - (2cθ - cα) = 2cα - 2cθ = 2(cα - cθ). So D = sα * 2(cα - cθ) = 2 sα (cα - cθ). Since cα - cθ = cos(σ-δ) - cos(σ+δ) = 2 sinσ sinδ? Actually, cos(σ-δ) - cos(σ+δ) = 2 sinσ sinδ (since cos(A-B)-cos(A+B)=2 sinA sinB). So cα - cθ = 2 sinσ sinδ = 2 sσ sδ. Thus, D = 2 sα * 2 sσ sδ = 4 sα sσ sδ.

But sα = sin(σ-δ) = sσ cδ - cσ sδ. So D = 4 (sσ cδ - cσ sδ) sσ sδ = 4 sσ sδ (sσ cδ - cσ sδ). That is fine.

Now, solve for A:

A = det( [ - (e+f)   sα ; - (B·B + e f)  sα ] ) / D = ( (-(e+f))*sα - sα*(-(B·B+e f)) ) / D = sα ( - (e+f) + (B·B+e f) ) / D = sα ( B·B + e f - e - f ) / D.

Thus, A = sα ( B·B + e f - e - f ) / D.

Similarly, B = det( [ cα   -(e+f); B_x   -(B·B+e f) ] ) / D = ( cα * (-(B·B+e f)) - (-(e+f)) * B_x ) / D = ( -cα (B·B+e f) + (e+f) B_x ) / D = ( (e+f) B_x - cα (B·B+e f) ) / D.

Now, we need to check if A^2 = 4 e f.

Since we have expressions, we can compute A in terms of known quantities. Let's compute B·B + e f - e - f.

First, compute e+f:

e+f = 2cθ cδ / cσ -1 + 2cθ cσ / cδ -1 = 2cθ (cδ/cσ + cσ/cδ) - 2 = 2cθ ( (cδ^2 + cσ^2)/(cσ cδ) ) - 2 = 2cθ ( (cσ^2 + cδ^2)/(cσ cδ) ) - 2.

But note that cσ^2 + cδ^2 = 1 - sσ^2 + 1 - sδ^2 = 2 - (sσ^2+sδ^2). Not sure.

Now, compute e f:

e f = (2cθ cδ / cσ -1)(2cθ cσ / cδ -1) = (2cθ cδ / cσ)(2cθ cσ / cδ) - (2cθ cδ / cσ) - (2cθ cσ / cδ) + 1 = (4cθ^2) - 2cθ (cδ/cσ + cσ/cδ) + 1.

Thus, e f = 4cθ^2 - 2cθ (cδ/cσ + cσ/cδ) + 1.

Now, compute B·B = 4cθ^2 -4cθ cα + 1.

So B·B + e f = (4cθ^2 -4cθ cα +1) + (4cθ^2 -2cθ (cδ/cσ + cσ/cδ) +1) = 8cθ^2 -4cθ cα -2cθ (cδ/cσ + cσ/cδ) + 2.

Then B·B + e f - e - f = 8cθ^2 -4cθ cα -2cθ (cδ/cσ + cσ/cδ) + 2 - (2cθ (cδ/cσ + cσ/cδ) - 2) = 8cθ^2 -4cθ cα -4cθ (cδ/cσ + cσ/cδ) + 4.

So B·B + e f - e - f = 4[2cθ^2 - cθ cα - cθ (cδ/cσ + cσ/cδ) + 1].

Now, we also have D = 4 sα sσ sδ. So A = sα * (B·B+ef-e-f) / D = sα * (4[2cθ^2 - cθ cα - cθ (cδ/cσ + cσ/cδ) + 1]) / (4 sα sσ sδ) = [2cθ^2 - cθ cα - cθ (cδ/cσ + cσ/cδ) + 1] / (sσ sδ). So A = (1 + 2cθ^2 - cθ cα - cθ (cδ/cσ + cσ/cδ)) / (sσ sδ).

Now, we need to compute e f. We have e f = 4cθ^2 - 2cθ (cδ/cσ + cσ/cδ) + 1.

We need to prove that A^2 = 4 e f.

Thus, we need to show:

[1 + 2cθ^2 - cθ cα - cθ (cδ/cσ + cσ/cδ)]^2 = 4 sσ^2 sδ^2 (4cθ^2 - 2cθ (cδ/cσ + cσ/cδ) + 1).

This is a trigonometric identity. Let's simplify using known relations between cα, cθ, cσ, cδ, sσ, sδ.

We have:

cα = cσ cδ + sσ sδ
cθ = cσ cδ - sσ sδ
sα = sσ cδ - cσ sδ

We also have:

cδ/cσ + cσ/cδ = (cδ^2 + cσ^2)/(cσ cδ) = (cσ^2 + cδ^2)/(cσ cδ). Note that cσ^2 + cδ^2 = 1 - sσ^2 + 1 - sδ^2 = 2 - (sσ^2+sδ^2). Also, sσ sδ appears.

Now, compute the numerator N = 1 + 2cθ^2 - cθ cα - cθ (cδ/cσ + cσ/cδ). Let's compute each term in terms of σ, δ.

First, cθ^2 = (cσ cδ - sσ sδ)^2 = cσ^2 cδ^2 - 2cσ cδ sσ sδ + sσ^2 sδ^2.
So 2cθ^2 = 2cσ^2 cδ^2 - 4cσ cδ sσ sδ + 2sσ^2 sδ^2.

Next, cθ cα = (cσ cδ - sσ sδ)(cσ cδ + sσ sδ) = (cσ cδ)^2 - (sσ sδ)^2 = cσ^2 cδ^2 - sσ^2 sδ^2.

Next, cθ (cδ/cσ + cσ/cδ) = cθ * (cδ/cσ + cσ/cδ) = (cσ cδ - sσ sδ)*(cδ/cσ + cσ/cδ) = (cσ cδ)*(cδ/cσ) + (cσ cδ)*(cσ/cδ) - (sσ sδ)*(cδ/cσ) - (sσ sδ)*(cσ/cδ) = cδ^2 + cσ^2 - sσ sδ*(cδ/cσ) - sσ sδ*(cσ/cδ). So = cσ^2 + cδ^2 - sσ sδ (cδ/cσ + cσ/cδ).

Now, compute N = 1 + 2cθ^2 - cθ cα - cθ (cδ/cσ + cσ/cδ) = 1 + (2cσ^2 cδ^2 -4cσ cδ sσ sδ +2sσ^2 sδ^2) - (cσ^2 cδ^2 - sσ^2 sδ^2) - (cσ^2 + cδ^2 - sσ sδ (cδ/cσ + cσ/cδ)).

Simplify:

N = 1 + 2cσ^2 cδ^2 -4cσ cδ sσ sδ +2sσ^2 sδ^2 - cσ^2 cδ^2 + sσ^2 sδ^2 - cσ^2 - cδ^2 + sσ sδ (cδ/cσ + cσ/cδ)
= 1 + (2cσ^2 cδ^2 - cσ^2 cδ^2) + (-4cσ cδ sσ sδ) + (2sσ^2 sδ^2 + sσ^2 sδ^2) - cσ^2 - cδ^2 + sσ sδ (cδ/cσ + cσ/cδ)
= 1 + cσ^2 cδ^2 -4cσ cδ sσ sδ +3sσ^2 sδ^2 - cσ^2 - cδ^2 + sσ sδ (cδ/cσ + cσ/cδ).

Now, note that cσ^2 cδ^2 - cσ^2 - cδ^2 = cσ^2 (cδ^2 -1) - cδ^2 = -cσ^2 sδ^2 - cδ^2. So that part is -cσ^2 sδ^2 - cδ^2.

Thus, N = 1 - cσ^2 sδ^2 - cδ^2 -4cσ cδ sσ sδ +3sσ^2 sδ^2 + sσ sδ (cδ/cσ + cσ/cδ).

Now, combine terms with sσ sδ: we have -4cσ cδ sσ sδ + sσ sδ (cδ/cσ + cσ/cδ) = sσ sδ [ -4cσ cδ + cδ/cσ + cσ/cδ ].

So N = 1 - cσ^2 sδ^2 - cδ^2 +3sσ^2 sδ^2 + sσ sδ [ -4cσ cδ + cδ/cσ + cσ/cδ ].

Now, we need to simplify N further. Let's express everything in terms of sσ, cσ, sδ, cδ. Also, note that sσ^2 = 1 - cσ^2, sδ^2 = 1 - cδ^2.

Compute 1 - cσ^2 sδ^2 - cδ^2 +3sσ^2 sδ^2 = 1 - cσ^2 sδ^2 - cδ^2 +3(1 - cσ^2)(1 - cδ^2) = 1 - cσ^2 sδ^2 - cδ^2 +3(1 - cσ^2 - cδ^2 + cσ^2 cδ^2) = 1 - cσ^2 sδ^2 - cδ^2 +3 -3cσ^2 -3cδ^2 +3cσ^2 cδ^2 = 4 - cσ^2 sδ^2 -4cσ^2 -4cδ^2 +3cσ^2 cδ^2. But note that cσ^2 sδ^2 = cσ^2 (1 - cδ^2) = cσ^2 - cσ^2 cδ^2. So then:

4 - (cσ^2 - cσ^2 cδ^2) -4cσ^2 -4cδ^2 +3cσ^2 cδ^2 = 4 - cσ^2 + cσ^2 cδ^2 -4cσ^2 -4cδ^2 +3cσ^2 cδ^2 = 4 -5cσ^2 -4cδ^2 +4cσ^2 cδ^2.

Thus, N = 4 -5cσ^2 -4cδ^2 +4cσ^2 cδ^2 + sσ sδ [ -4cσ cδ + cδ/cσ + cσ/cδ ].

Now, we need to show that N^2 = 4 sσ^2 sδ^2 (4cθ^2 - 2cθ (cδ/cσ + cσ/cδ) + 1). But this looks messy. We have circles Ω and Γ intersecting at A and B. Centers M, N. Line MN meets Ω at C and Γ at D, with order C-M-N-D. So M is between C and N, N between M and D.

Given the algebraic complexity, maybe we can complete the algebraic verification we started. We have A = (1 + 2cθ^2 - cθ cα - cθ (cδ/cσ + cσ/cδ)) / (sσ sδ). Let's compute that expression more simply using the relations.

We have:

cθ = cos(σ+δ) = cσ cδ - sσ sδ
cα = cos(σ-δ) = cσ cδ + sσ sδ
cθ cα = (cσ cδ)^2 - (sσ sδ)^2 = cσ^2 cδ^2 - sσ^2 sδ^2
2cθ^2 = 2(cσ^2 cδ^2 - 2cσ cδ sσ sδ + sσ^2 sδ^2) = 2cσ^2 cδ^2 - 4cσ cδ sσ sδ + 2sσ^2 sδ^2
So 1 + 2cθ^2 - cθ cα = 1 + (2cσ^2 cδ^2 - 4cσ cδ sσ sδ + 2sσ^2 sδ^2) - (cσ^2 cδ^2 - sσ^2 sδ^2) = 1 + cσ^2 cδ^2 - 4cσ cδ sσ sδ + 3sσ^2 sδ^2.

Now, subtract cθ (cδ/cσ + cσ/cδ). We have cθ (cδ/cσ + cσ/cδ) = (cσ cδ - sσ sδ)(cδ/cσ + cσ/cδ) = cδ^2 + cσ^2 - sσ sδ (cδ/cσ + cσ/cδ). So:

N = 1 + cσ^2 cδ^2 - 4cσ cδ sσ sδ + 3sσ^2 sδ^2 - (cδ^2 + cσ^2) + sσ sδ (cδ/cσ + cσ/cδ)
= 1 + cσ^2 cδ^2 - 4cσ cδ sσ sδ + 3sσ^2 sδ^2 - cσ^2 - cδ^2 + sσ sδ (cδ/cσ + cσ/cδ).

Now, cσ^2 cδ^2 - cσ^2 - cδ^2 = -cσ^2 (1-cδ^2) - cδ^2 = -cσ^2 sδ^2 - cδ^2. So:

N = 1 - cσ^2 sδ^2 - cδ^2 - 4cσ cδ sσ sδ + 3sσ^2 sδ^2 + sσ sδ (cδ/cσ + cσ/cδ).

Now, combine the sσ sδ terms: -4cσ cδ sσ sδ + sσ sδ (cδ/cσ + cσ/cδ) = sσ sδ ( -4cσ cδ + cδ/cσ + cσ/cδ ). And the remaining: 1 - cσ^2 sδ^2 - cδ^2 + 3sσ^2 sδ^2.

But earlier we had 1 - cσ^2 sδ^2 - cδ^2 + 3sσ^2 sδ^2 = 4 -5cσ^2 -4cδ^2 +4cσ^2 cδ^2? Let's recalc: Using sσ^2=1-cσ^2, sδ^2=1-cδ^2, then 1 - cσ^2(1-cδ^2) - cδ^2 + 3(1-cσ^2)(1-cδ^2) = 1 - cσ^2 + cσ^2 cδ^2 - cδ^2 + 3(1 - cσ^2 - cδ^2 + cσ^2 cδ^2) = 1 - cσ^2 + cσ^2 cδ^2 - cδ^2 + 3 - 3cσ^2 - 3cδ^2 + 3cσ^2 cδ^2 = 4 -4cσ^2 -4cδ^2 +4cσ^2 cδ^2. So N = 4 -4cσ^2 -4cδ^2 +4cσ^2 cδ^2 + sσ sδ ( -4cσ cδ + cδ/cσ + cσ/cδ ). So N = 4(1 - cσ^2 - cδ^2 + cσ^2 cδ^2) + sσ sδ ( -4cσ cδ + cδ/cσ + cσ/cδ ). But 1 - cσ^2 - cδ^2 + cσ^2 cδ^2 = (1 - cσ^2)(1 - cδ^2) = sσ^2 sδ^2. So N = 4 sσ^2 sδ^2 + sσ sδ ( -4cσ cδ + cδ/cσ + cσ/cδ ). So N = sσ sδ [ 4 sσ sδ - 4cσ cδ + cδ/cσ + cσ/cδ ].

Thus, A = N / (sσ sδ) = 4 sσ sδ - 4cσ cδ + cδ/cσ + cσ/cδ.

So A = 4(sσ sδ - cσ cδ) + (cδ/cσ + cσ/cδ). But note that sσ sδ - cσ cδ = - cos(σ+δ) = -cθ. Because cos(σ+δ)=cσ cδ - sσ sδ. So sσ sδ - cσ cδ = -cθ. Thus, A = 4(-cθ) + (cδ/cσ + cσ/cδ) = -4cθ + (cδ/cσ + cσ/cδ).

Great! So A = (cδ/cσ + cσ/cδ) - 4cθ. That is much simpler.

Now, recall that e f = 4cθ^2 - 2cθ (cδ/cσ + cσ/cδ) + 1. And we need to show that A^2 = 4 e f.

Compute A^2 = (cδ/cσ + cσ/cδ - 4cθ)^2.

Now, let’s denote X = cδ/cσ + cσ/cδ, and Y = cθ. Then A = X - 4Y. And e f = 4Y^2 - 2X Y + 1. So we need to verify that (X - 4Y)^2 = 4(4Y^2 - 2XY + 1). Expand left: X^2 - 8XY + 16Y^2. Right: 16Y^2 - 8XY + 4. So the equality reduces to X^2 - 8XY + 16Y^2 = 16Y^2 - 8XY + 4 => X^2 = 4. So we need to prove that X = cδ/cσ + cσ/cδ = 2. That is, (cδ/cσ + cσ/cδ) = 2.

But is that true? cδ and cσ are cosines of some angles. They are not necessarily equal. But note that δ = (θ-α)/2 and σ = (α+θ)/2. So δ and σ are not independent. Let's compute σ and δ: α=30°, θ=45°, so σ=(α+θ)/2=37.5°, δ=(θ-α)/2=7.5°. Then sinσ=sin37.5°≈0.6088, cosσ=cos37.5°≈0.7934, sinδ=sin7.5°≈0.1305, cosδ=cos7.5°≈0.9914. Then m_y = (sinσ cosθ)/cosσ = (0.6088*0.7071)/0.7934=0.4305/0.7934=0.542. n_y = - (sinδ cosθ)/cosδ = - (0.1305*0.7071)/0.9914 = -0.0923/0.9914=-0.0931. So M=(0.7071,0.542), N=(0.7071,-0.0931). Good.

Now, compute H: orthocenter of triangle P=0, M, N. We computed y_H=0 because N-M = (0, -0.6351). So H lies on x-axis. Then H·N = M·N =? M·N = 0.7071*0.7071 + 0.542*(-0.0931)=0.5 -0.0505=0.4495. N=(0.7071,-0.0931). So H·N = x_H*0.7071 + y_H*(-0.0931)=0.7071 x_H =0.4495 => x_H=0.4495/0.7071=0.636. So H=(0.636,0). So line L through H parallel to AP: AP direction = A = (0.8660,0.5). So L: (0.636+0.866t, 0+0.5t). In general form: a normal vector to L is perpendicular to (0.866,0.5). One such is (0.5, -0.866). So equation: 0.5(x-0.636) -0.866(y-0)=0 => 0.5x -0.318 -0.866y=0 => 0.5x -0.866y =0.318. That is the line.

Now, compute circle through B, E, F.

We have B: reflection of A across line CD. Line CD: vertical line x=0.7071. So B = (2*0.7071 - 0.8660, 0.5) = (1.4142-0.8660, 0.5) = (0.5482, 0.5). Good.

E: second intersection of line through origin and A with Ω. We computed t_E = 2(A·M)-1 = 2*(0.8660*0.7071+0.5*0.542)-1=2*(0.6124+0.271)-1=2*0.8834-1=0.7668. So E = 0.7668*(0.8660,0.5) = (0.663, 0.3834). Similarly, F = 0.1317*(0.8660,0.5) = (0.1142, 0.06585). So points:

B = (0.5482, 0.5)
E = (0.663, 0.3834)
F = (0.1142, 0.06585)

Now, find circumcenter O of triangle BEF. We computed O≈(0.3869,0.2286). Radius R = distance to B: sqrt((0.5482-0.3869)^2+(0.5-0.2286)^2)= sqrt(0.1613^2+0.2714^2)= sqrt(0.0260+0.0737)= sqrt(0.0997)=0.3155.

Now, distance from O to line L: |0.5*0.3869 -0.866*0.2286 -0.318| / sqrt(0.5^2+0.866^2) = |0.19345 -0.1978 -0.318| = | -0.32235| =0.32235. Since sqrt(0.5^2+0.866^2)=1 (since 0.5^2+0.866^2=0.25+0.75=1). So distance =0.32235. This is greater than radius 0.3155, so the line L does not intersect the circle (it's outside). Therefore, the line through H parallel to AP is not tangent; it's actually external (no intersection). But the problem statement says "Prove that the line through H parallel to AP is tangent to the circumcircle of triangle BEF." So either our numeric example is not a valid configuration because of the order condition? Let's check the order condition: C, M, N, D lie on MN in that order. In our example, points on vertical line: C at y=0.7071, M at y=0.542, N at y=-0.0931, D at y=-0.7071. So indeed, C-M-N-D in that order (top to bottom). So order holds. Also, r<R holds. So this seems to be a valid configuration. But the conclusion fails. So maybe our identification of E and F is incorrect? In the problem, AP meets Ω again at E. Since A is on Ω, and line AP is the line through A and P. In our coordinate system, P=0, A=(0.8660,0.5). So line AP is the ray from origin to A. That line is indeed the line through A and P. So E is the other intersection of that line with Ω. We computed E using formula t_E = 2(A·M)-1. That is correct because the circle Ω has center M, and line through origin and A: we solved for t such that |tA - M| = |A-M|. The quadratic gave t=1 and t=2(A·M)-1. So that's correct. Similarly for F. So E and F are correct.

Thus, the numeric example suggests that the line through H parallel to AP is not tangent to (BEF). But wait, we also computed the rotated version (where AP is horizontal) and found tangency. So maybe the property is not invariant under rotation because the definition of H depends on the triangle PMN, and rotating the whole configuration rotates H as well. So if the statement holds in one orientation, it should hold in any orientation. But we have a numeric counterexample in the orientation where A is at 30°. So either our numeric test in that orientation is wrong, or the rotated orientation test is wrong, or the property is not invariant? Let's test the rotated orientation numerically again, but more precisely.

In the rotated orientation, we had A=(1,0), C=(cos15°, sin15°)≈(0.9659258,0.258819), D=(cos(-75°), sin(-75°))≈(0.258819,-0.9659258). We computed B≈(0.7248,0.1596). Distance from O to this line = |0.000626 - (-0.3178)|=0.3184, which is slightly larger than radius 0.3178. The discrepancy is due to rounding; but let's compute more precisely.

We should compute with higher precision using exact trigonometric values where possible.

Let’s use exact values: α=30°, θ=45°. Then we have cos30°=√3/2≈0.86602540378, sin30°=0.5. cos45°=√2/2≈0.70710678119, sin45°=√2/2≈0.70710678119. So A=(√3/2, 0.5). C=(√2/2, √2/2). D=(√2/2, -√2/2). Now, compute M: M is intersection of line CD (x=√2/2) with perpendicular bisector of AC. Compute AC: A(√3/2,0.5), C(√2/2,√2/2). Midpoint: ((√3/2+√2/2)/2, (0.5+√2/2)/2) = ((√3+√2)/4, (1+√2)/4). Direction of AC: (√2/2 - √3/2, √2/2 - 0.5) = ((√2-√3)/2, (√2-1)/2). A perpendicular vector: ( (√2-1)/2, -(√2-√3)/2 )? Because dot: ((√2-√3)/2)*( (√2-1)/2 ) + ((√2-1)/2)*(-(√2-√3)/2) = 0. So perpendicular bisector: Y = midpoint + s * ( (√2-1)/2, -(√2-√3)/2 ). We need intersection with x=√2/2. Write param: x = (√3+√2)/4 + s*(√2-1)/2 = √2/2. Solve for s: s*(√2-1)/2 = √2/2 - (√3+√2)/4 = (2√2 - √3 - √2)/4 = (√2 - √3)/4. So s = ((√2 - √3)/4) * 2/(√2-1)) = (√2 - √3)/(2(√2-1)). Then y_M = (1+√2)/4 + s * (-(√2-√3)/2). Compute s = (√2-√3)/(2(√2-1)). Multiply numerator and denominator by (√2+1) to rationalize: (√2-√3)(√2+1)/(2(2-1)) = (√2-√3)(√2+1)/2. So s = ((√2-√3)(√2+1))/2. Then y_M = (1+√2)/4 - (1/2)*((√2-√3)(√2+1)/2)*(√2-√3)/2? Wait, careful: y_M = (1+√2)/4 + s * (-(√2-√3)/2) = (1+√2)/4 - (√2-√3)/2 * s. So y_M = (1+√2)/4 - (√2-√3)/2 * ((√2-√3)(√2+1)/2) = (1+√2)/4 - ((√2-√3)^2 (√2+1))/4. So y_M = [ (1+√2) - (√2-√3)^2 (√2+1) ] / 4 = (√2+1)[1 - (√2-√3)^2] / 4. Now, (√2-√3)^2 = 2 + 3 - 2√6 = 5 - 2√6. So 1 - (5-2√6) = -4 + 2√6 = 2(√6 - 2). So y_M = (√2+1)*2(√6-2)/4 = (√2+1)(√6-2)/2. Numerically, √2+1≈2.41421356237, √6-2≈2.44948974278-2=0.44948974278, product≈1.085, half≈0.5425. So y_M≈0.5425. Good.

Now, N: intersection of line CD with perpendicular bisector of AD. AD: A(√3/2,0.5), D(√2/2,-√2/2). Midpoint: ((√3/2+√2/2)/2, (0.5-√2/2)/2) = ((√3+√2)/4, (1-√2)/4). Direction of AD: (√2/2 - √3/2, -√2/2 - 0.5) = ((√2-√3)/2, -(√2+1)/2). A perpendicular vector: ( -(√2+1)/2, (√2-√3)/2 ) (since dot: ((√2-√3)/2)*(-(√2+1)/2) + (-(√2+1)/2)*((√2-√3)/2) = -((√2-√3)(√2+1))/4 - ((√2+1)(√2-√3))/4 = -((√2-√3)(√2+1))/2? Actually, that's not zero. Let's compute properly: We need a vector (p,q) such that ((√2-√3)/2)*p + (-(√2+1)/2)*q = 0 => (√2-√3)p = (√2+1)q => q = ((√2-√3)/(√2+1)) p. Set x = (√3+√2)/4 + s*(√2+1) = √2/2 => s*(√2+1) = √2/2 - (√3+√2)/4 = (2√2 - √3 - √2)/4 = (√2 - √3)/4 => s = (√2-√3)/(4(√2+1)). So y_N = (1-√2)/4 + ((√2-√3)(√2-1)/4)*(√2-√3) = (1-√2)/4 + ((√2-√3)^2 (√2-1))/4 = (1-√2 + (√2-√3)^2 (√2-1))/4. Now, (√2-√3)^2 = 5-2√6. So y_N = (1-√2 + (5-2√6)(√2-1))/4. Compute (5-2√6)(√2-1) = (5-2√6)√2 - (5-2√6) = 5√2 -2√12 -5 +2√6 = 5√2 -4√3 -5 +2√6. So y_N = (1-√2 + 5√2 -4√3 -5 +2√6)/4 = ( -4 +4√2 -4√3 +2√6 )/4 = ( -2 +2√2 -2√3 +√6 )/2. Numerically, √2≈1.4142, √3≈1.7321, √6≈2.4495, so -2+2.8284-3.4641+2.4495 = -0.2882? Actually, compute: -2+2.8284=0.8284, -3.4641 gives -2.6357, +2.4495 gives -0.1862, half gives -0.0931. So y_N≈-0.0931. So N=(√2/2, y_N) with y_N = (-2+2√2-2√3+√6)/2.

Now, compute H: orthocenter of triangle P=0, M=(√2/2, y_M), N=(√2/2, y_N). As before, N-M = (0, y_N - y_M). So H lies on x-axis. H·N = M·N. M·N = (√2/2)*(√2/2) + y_M y_N = 1/2 + y_M y_N. Compute y_M y_N. y_M = (√2+1)(√6-2)/2, y_N = (-2+2√2-2√3+√6)/2. Let's compute y_M y_N numerically: y_M≈0.5425, y_N≈-0.0931, product≈-0.0505. So M·N≈0.5-0.0505=0.4495. N=(√2/2, y_N)≈(0.7071,-0.0931). So H=(x_H,0) with x_H = (M·N)/ (√2/2) = 0.4495/0.7071=0.636. So H=(0.636,0). Good.

Now, compute E and F.

A=(√3/2, 0.5). M=(√2/2, y_M). A·M = (√3/2)*(√2/2) + 0.5*y_M = (√6)/4 + 0.5*y_M. Numerically, √6/4≈0.61237, 0.5*y_M≈0.27125, sum≈0.88362. So t_E = 2*(A·M)-1 = 2*0.88362-1=0.76724. So E = t_E A = 0.76724*(√3/2,0.5) = (0.76724*0.8660254, 0.76724*0.5) = (0.6635,0.38362). F: A·N = (√3/2)*(√2/2) + 0.5*y_N = √6/4 + 0.5*y_N = 0.61237 +0.5*(-0.0931)=0.61237-0.04655=0.56582. So t_F = 2*0.56582-1=0.13164. So F = (0.13164*0.8660254, 0.13164*0.5) = (0.1141,0.06582). So points.

Now, circle through B, E, F. B=(√2 - √3/2, 0.5) ≈ (1.41421356237-0.86602540378=0.54818815859, 0.5). We need O.

Let’s compute O exactly using formulas. Since we have exact coordinates, we can derive symbolic expressions. However, we already have numeric. Let's compute O more precisely.

We can use the fact that the circle (BEF) is the circumcircle of triangle BEF. We can compute its equation using complex numbers or coordinate geometry with exact values.

We have coordinates:

A = (a_x, a_y) = (√3/2, 1/2)

F = (f_x, f_y) = t_F (a_x, a_y) with t_F = 2(a·N)-1, N = (√2/2, y_N) with y_N = (-2+2√2-2√3+√6)/2

We can compute O as intersection of perpendicular bisectors. However, maybe we can use the earlier algebraic derivation that in the coordinate system with A on x-axis (after rotation), we have that the line through H parallel to AP is horizontal, and we want to prove it is tangent. We have O = (0.3869, 0.2286) approximate. Let's compute O more precisely.

We can compute O using the formula we derived earlier for the circumcenter of BEF in the rotated system, but we need O in the original system. However, we can compute O directly using the coordinates.

We have B=(b_x,b_y), E=(e_x,e_y), F=(f_x,f_y). Let's compute O by solving for the intersection of perpendicular bisectors of BE and BF, or using linear equations.

Since we have numeric, let's use high precision calculator (mentally or with exact expressions). Let's use exact values.

Define:

a_x = √3/2, a_y = 1/2.
b_x = √2 - √3/2, b_y = 1/2.
M = (c_x, y_M) with c_x = √2/2, y_M = (√2+1)(√6-2)/2.
N = (c_x, y_N) with y_N = (-2+2√2-2√3+√6)/2.
t_E = 2(a·M)-1, t_F = 2(a·N)-1.
Compute a·M = a_x c_x + a_y y_M = (√3/2)*(√2/2) + (1/2)*y_M = √6/4 + y_M/2.
Similarly, a·N = √6/4 + y_N/2.
So t_E = 2(√6/4 + y_M/2)-1 = √6/2 + y_M -1.
t_F = √6/2 + y_N -1.

Thus, E = t_E (a_x, a_y) = (t_E a_x, t_E a_y) = ( (√6/2 + y_M -1) * √3/2, (√6/2 + y_M -1) * 1/2 ).
Similarly, F = ( (√6/2 + y_N -1) * √3/2, (√6/2 + y_N -1) * 1/2 ).

Now, y_M and y_N are known. Let's compute y_M exactly:

y_M = (√2+1)(√6-2)/2. Expand: (√2+1)(√6-2) = √2*√6 -2√2 + √6 -2 = √12 -2√2 + √6 -2 = 2√3 -2√2 + √6 -2. So y_M = (2√3 -2√2 + √6 -2)/2 = √3 -√2 + (√6)/2 -1. So y_M = √3 - √2 + (√6)/2 - 1.

Similarly, y_N = (-2+2√2-2√3+√6)/2 = -1 + √2 - √3 + (√6)/2. So y_N = √2 - √3 + (√6)/2 -1.

Thus, y_M = √3 - √2 + (√6)/2 -1, y_N = √2 - √3 + (√6)/2 -1.

Now, compute t_E = √6/2 + y_M -1 = √6/2 + (√3 - √2 + √6/2 -1) -1 = √3 - √2 + √6 -2. So t_E = √3 - √2 + √6 - 2. Numerically, √3≈1.73205, √2≈1.41421, √6≈2.44949, so t_E≈1.73205-1.41421+2.44949-2=0.76723. Good.

t_F = √6/2 + y_N -1 = √6/2 + (√2 - √3 + √6/2 -1) -1 = √2 - √3 + √6 -2. So t_F = √2 - √3 + √6 - 2. Numerically, 1.41421-1.73205+2.44949-2=-0.168? Wait, compute: 1.41421-1.73205=-0.31784, +2.44949=2.13165, -2=0.13165. So t_F≈0.13165. Good.

Now, we have exact coordinates:

B = (√2 - √3/2, 1/2). Let's denote b_x = √2 - √3/2, b_y = 1/2.
E = (t_E a_x, t_E a_y) = ( (√3 - √2 + √6 - 2) * √3/2, (√3 - √2 + √6 - 2) * 1/2 ) = ( (√3(√3 - √2 + √6 - 2))/2, (√3 - √2 + √6 - 2)/2 ).
Compute √3(√3 - √2 + √6 - 2) = 3 - √6 + √18 - 2√3 = 3 - √6 + 3√2 - 2√3. So e_x = (3 - √6 + 3√2 - 2√3)/2. e_y = (√3 - √2 + √6 - 2)/2.

F = (t_F a_x, t_F a_y) = ( (√2 - √3 + √6 - 2) * √3/2, (√2 - √3 + √6 - 2) * 1/2 ) = ( (√3(√2 - √3 + √6 - 2))/2, (√2 - √3 + √6 - 2)/2 ). Compute √3(√2 - √3 + √6 - 2) = √6 - 3 + √18 - 2√3 = √6 - 3 + 3√2 - 2√3. So f_x = (√6 - 3 + 3√2 - 2√3)/2. f_y = (√2 - √3 + √6 - 2)/2.

Now, we need circumcenter O of triangle BEF. Since we have exact coordinates, we can compute O by solving the linear system for the perpendicular bisector of EF (which is vertical line through midpoint of EF) and the perpendicular bisector of BE.

First, find midpoint of EF: ( (e_x+f_x)/2, (e_y+f_y)/2 ). Compute e_x+f_x:

e_x = (3 - √6 + 3√2 - 2√3)/2
f_x = (√6 - 3 + 3√2 - 2√3)/2
Sum = (3 - √6 + 3√2 - 2√3 + √6 - 3 + 3√2 - 2√3)/2 = ( (3-3) + (-√6+√6) + (3√2+3√2) + (-2√3-2√3) )/2 = (6√2 - 4√3)/2 = 3√2 - 2√3. So e_x+f_x = 3√2 - 2√3. So midpoint x-coordinate = (e_x+f_x)/2 = (3√2 - 2√3)/2. So the perpendicular bisector of EF is the vertical line x = (3√2 - 2√3)/2. So O = (X, y_O) with X = (3√2 - 2√3)/2.

Now, compute X exactly: (3√2 - 2√3)/2. Numerically, 3*1.4142=4.2426, 2*1.7321=3.4642, difference=0.7784, half=0.3892. That matches our earlier approximate 0.3869? Slight difference due to rounding. So X=0.3892? Let's compute more precisely: √2≈1.4142135623730951, √3≈1.7320508075688772, so 3√2≈4.242640687119285, 2√3≈3.4641016151377544, difference=0.7785390719815306, half=0.3892695359907653. So X≈0.38927. Our earlier O_x was 0.3869, so that was off a bit. Good.

Now, we need y_O from the condition that O is equidistant from B and E. Compute O - B and O - E squared.

Let O = (X, y). Then:

(O - B)^2 = (X - b_x)^2 + (y - 1/2)^2.
(O - E)^2 = (X - e_x)^2 + (y - e_y)^2.

Set equal: (X - b_x)^2 + (y - 1/2)^2 = (X - e_x)^2 + (y - e_y)^2.

Expand: (X^2 - 2X b_x + b_x^2) + (y^2 - y + 1/4) = (X^2 - 2X e_x + e_x^2) + (y^2 - 2y e_y + e_y^2). Cancel X^2, y^2: -2X b_x + b_x^2 - y + 1/4 = -2X e_x + e_x^2 - 2y e_y + e_y^2. Bring terms: -2X b_x + b_x^2 - y + 1/4 + 2X e_x - e_x^2 + 2y e_y - e_y^2 = 0 => 2X(e_x - b_x) + (b_x^2 - e_x^2) + y(2e_y - 1) + (1/4 - e_y^2) = 0.

Thus, y(2e_y - 1) = -2X(e_x - b_x) - (b_x^2 - e_x^2) - (1/4 - e_y^2) = -2X(e_x - b_x) - (b_x - e_x)(b_x + e_x) - (1/4 - e_y^2).

But we can compute directly.

Let’s compute all terms exactly.

First, b_x = √2 - √3/2 = (2√2 - √3)/2? Actually, √2 = (2√2)/2, so b_x = (2√2 - √3)/2. So b_x = (2√2 - √3)/2.

e_x = (3 - √6 + 3√2 - 2√3)/2.

X = (3√2 - 2√3)/2.

Now, compute e_x - b_x:

e_x - b_x = [ (3 - √6 + 3√2 - 2√3) - (2√2 - √3) ] / 2 = [ 3 - √6 + 3√2 - 2√3 - 2√2 + √3 ] / 2 = [ 3 - √6 + (3√2-2√2) + (-2√3+√3) ] / 2 = [ 3 - √6 + √2 - √3 ] / 2.

So e_x - b_x = (3 - √6 + √2 - √3)/2.

Now, X - b_x = (3√2 - 2√3)/2 - (2√2 - √3)/2 = (3√2 - 2√3 - 2√2 + √3)/2 = (√2 - √3)/2.

Thus, X - b_x = (√2 - √3)/2.

Also, X - e_x = (3√2 - 2√3)/2 - (3 - √6 + 3√2 - 2√3)/2 = (3√2 - 2√3 - 3 + √6 - 3√2 + 2√3)/2 = (-3 + √6)/2 = (√6 - 3)/2.

Now, b_x^2 and e_x^2. Compute b_x^2:

b_x = (2√2 - √3)/2, so b_x^2 = ( (2√2 - √3)^2 )/4 = (8 + 3 - 4√6)/4 = (11 - 4√6)/4.

e_x^2: e_x = (3 - √6 + 3√2 - 2√3)/2. Let’s denote numerator: N = 3 - √6 + 3√2 - 2√3. Square N: N^2 = (3 - √6 + 3√2 - 2√3)^2. This might be messy. But we can also compute using the relation we know: E is on the circle Ω, so |E-M| = r, but that's not helpful.

Alternatively, we can compute y_O using the formula for the circumcenter in terms of coordinates. Another approach: Since we have exact expressions, we can compute the circle equation using the determinant method. The circumcenter O can be found as the intersection of perpendicular bisectors, and we already have the vertical line x=X. So we just need to compute y such that O is equidistant from B and E. Let's compute using the condition: (O-B)^2 = (O-E)^2.

We have:

(O-B)^2 = (X - b_x)^2 + (y - 1/2)^2.
(O-E)^2 = (X - e_x)^2 + (y - e_y)^2.

Set equal: (X - b_x)^2 + (y - 1/2)^2 = (X - e_x)^2 + (y - e_y)^2.

We can compute (X - b_x)^2 = ((√2 - √3)/2)^2 = ( (√2 - √3)^2 )/4 = (2 + 3 - 2√6)/4 = (5 - 2√6)/4.

(X - e_x)^2 = ((√6 - 3)/2)^2 = ( (√6 - 3)^2 )/4 = (6 + 9 - 6√6)/4 = (15 - 6√6)/4 = (3(5 - 2√6))/4? Actually, 15-6√6 = 3(5-2√6). So (X-e_x)^2 = 3(5-2√6)/4.

Now, expand the equation:

(5-2√6)/4 + (y^2 - y + 1/4) = 3(5-2√6)/4 + (y^2 - 2y e_y + e_y^2). Cancel y^2: (5-2√6)/4 - y + 1/4 = 3(5-2√6)/4 - 2y e_y + e_y^2.

Bring terms: - y + 1/4 - (5-2√6)/4 = - 2y e_y + e_y^2 - 3(5-2√6)/4. Compute left: ( - y ) + (1/4 - (5-2√6)/4) = - y + ( (1 -5 +2√6)/4 ) = - y + ( (-4 +2√6)/4 ) = - y + ( (2√6 -4)/4 ) = - y + ( (√6 -2)/2 ). So left side = - y + (√6 -2)/2.

Thus, equation: - y + (√6 -2)/2 = - 2y e_y + e_y^2 - 3(5-2√6)/4.

Now, we need e_y. e_y = (√3 - √2 + √6 - 2)/2? Wait, earlier we had e_y = (√3 - √2 + √6 - 2)/2. Let's confirm: e_y = (√3 - √2 + √6 - 2)/2. That's correct. So e_y = (√3 - √2 + √6 - 2)/2.

Now, compute e_y^2: e_y^2 = ( (√3 - √2 + √6 - 2)^2 )/4.

We also need 2e_y = √3 - √2 + √6 - 2.

So 2e_y = √3 - √2 + √6 - 2.

Now, let's compute the right side: -2y e_y + e_y^2 - 3(5-2√6)/4 = -y (2e_y) + e_y^2 - 3(5-2√6)/4 = -y (√3 - √2 + √6 - 2) + e_y^2 - 3(5-2√6)/4.

Thus, the equation becomes:

- y + (√6 -2)/2 = -y (√3 - √2 + √6 - 2) + e_y^2 - 3(5-2√6)/4.

Bring terms with y: - y + y (√3 - √2 + √6 - 2) = e_y^2 - 3(5-2√6)/4 - (√6 -2)/2.

So y ( -1 + (√3 - √2 + √6 - 2) ) = y ( √3 - √2 + √6 - 3 ) = e_y^2 - 3(5-2√6)/4 - (√6 -2)/2.

Now, compute the coefficient: √3 - √2 + √6 - 3. Numerically, √3=1.73205, √2=1.41421, √6=2.44949, sum=1.73205-1.41421+2.44949=2.76733, minus 3 = -0.23267. So coefficient negative.

Now, we need e_y^2 and the constant. This is getting messy, but we can try to compute e_y^2 exactly.

Let’s compute e_y: e_y = (√3 - √2 + √6 - 2)/2. Let’s denote a = √3, b = √2, c = √6. Note that c = √3√2 = ab. So e_y = (a - b + ab - 2)/2. Compute e_y^2 = ( (a - b + ab - 2)^2 )/4.

We need to compute (a - b + ab - 2)^2. Expand:

Let’s compute: (a - b + ab - 2)^2 = (a - b + ab)^2 -4(a - b + ab) + 4.

Now, (a - b + ab)^2 = (a - b)^2 + 2(a - b)ab + (ab)^2 = (a^2 + b^2 -2ab) + 2ab(a - b) + a^2 b^2 = (3+2-2√6) + 2√6(√3-√2) + 3*2? Actually, a^2=3, b^2=2, ab=√6. So (a-b)^2 = 3+2-2√6 = 5-2√6. Then 2ab(a-b) = 2√6(√3-√2) = 2√6√3 - 2√6√2 = 2√18 - 2√12 = 6√2 - 4√3. And (ab)^2 = 6. So sum = (5-2√6) + (6√2-4√3) + 6 = 11 -2√6 +6√2 -4√3. So (a - b + ab)^2 = 11 -2√6 +6√2 -4√3.

Thus, (a - b + ab - 2)^2 = (11 -2√6 +6√2 -4√3) -4(a - b + ab) +4 = 11 -2√6 +6√2 -4√3 -4a +4b -4ab +4 = (11+4) -2√6 +6√2 -4√3 -4√3 +4√2 -4√6? Wait, careful: -4(a - b + ab) = -4a +4b -4ab. So then total = 11 -2√6 +6√2 -4√3 -4√3 +4√2 -4√6 +4? Actually, we have +4 from the last term, so constant: 11+4=15. Then terms: -2√6 -4√6 = -6√6. Then √2: 6√2+4√2=10√2. Then √3: -4√3 -4√3 = -8√3. So (a - b + ab - 2)^2 = 15 -6√6 +10√2 -8√3. Thus, e_y^2 = (15 -6√6 +10√2 -8√3)/4.

Now, compute the constant: e_y^2 - 3(5-2√6)/4 - (√6 -2)/2.

First, 3(5-2√6)/4 = (15 -6√6)/4.
Second, (√6 -2)/2 = (2√6 -4)/4? Actually, multiply numerator and denominator: (√6-2)/2 = (2√6-4)/4.

So constant = [15 -6√6 +10√2 -8√3]/4 - (15 -6√6)/4 - (2√6-4)/4 = combine first two: (15 -6√6 +10√2 -8√3 -15 +6√6)/4 = (10√2 -8√3)/4 = (5√2 -4√3)/2. Then subtract (2√6-4)/4: (5√2 -4√3)/2 - (2√6-4)/4 = (2(5√2-4√3) - (2√6-4))/4 = (10√2 -8√3 -2√6 +4)/4 = (10√2 -8√3 -2√6 +4)/4. So constant = (4 +10√2 -8√3 -2√6)/4 = (2 +5√2 -4√3 -√6)/2? Actually, divide numerator by 2: (2 +5√2 -4√3 -√6)/2. So constant = (2 +5√2 -4√3 -√6)/2.

Thus, the equation for y becomes:

y ( √3 - √2 + √6 - 3 ) = (2 +5√2 -4√3 -√6)/2.

Now, compute the coefficient: √3 - √2 + √6 - 3. Let's denote C = √3 - √2 + √6 - 3. We can compute C exactly. Numerically, C = 1.73205 -1.41421 +2.44949 -3 = -0.23267. So we have y = (2 +5√2 -4√3 -√6) / (2 (√3 - √2 + √6 - 3) ).

Now, we need to compute y_O exactly. Let's simplify y.

First, compute numerator N = 2 +5√2 -4√3 -√6.
Denominator D = 2(√3 - √2 + √6 - 3) = 2(√3 - √2 + √6 - 3).

We can try to rationalize or see if y simplifies to something like (√6-2)/2? We had earlier in the rotated system that y_O ≈ 0.000626, which is very small. In this system, we expect y_O to be small because the circumcenter is near the x-axis? Actually, in the original orientation, we found O≈(0.38927, y_O). Our earlier numeric O from approximate was (0.3869,0.2286), which is far from the x-axis. But that was from approximate calculations; we now have exact X and we can compute y_O exactly. Let's compute y_O numerically using the exact expression.

Compute √2≈1.4142135623730951, √3≈1.7320508075688772, √6≈2.449489742783178. Then numerator N = 2 +5*1.4142135623730951 -4*1.7320508075688772 -2.449489742783178 = 2 +7.0710678118654755 -6.928203230275509 -2.449489742783178 = 2 +7.0710678118654755 =9.0710678118654755, minus 6.928203230275509 =2.1428645815899665, minus 2.449489742783178 = -0.3066251611932115. So N≈ -0.306625. Denominator D = 2*(1.7320508075688772 -1.4142135623730951 +2.449489742783178 -3) = 2*(1.7320508075688772-1.4142135623730951=0.3178372451957821, +2.449489742783178=2.76732698797896, -3=-0.23267301202104) = -0.46534602404208. So y_O = N/D = (-0.306625)/(-0.465346)=0.6586? That is not small. That seems wrong because we expected y_O to be small from the rotated system? Wait, in the rotated system, we had y_O≈0.000626. In this system, we are not rotating; we are in the original orientation where AP is at 30°. So the circumcenter O is not near the x-axis; it's at y_O≈0.6586? That would be huge. But our earlier numeric O from approximate gave O≈(0.3869,0.2286). So there is a big discrepancy. Our exact calculation gave y_O≈0.6586, but earlier we had O_y≈0.2286. Which is correct? Let's recalc the exact expression for y_O more carefully.

We had: y (√3 - √2 + √6 - 3) = (2 +5√2 -4√3 -√6)/2. Let's compute the right side numerically again.

Compute 2 +5√2 -4√3 -√6 exactly:

5√2 = 5*1.4142135623730951 = 7.0710678118654755
4√3 = 4*1.7320508075688772 = 6.928203230275509

Subtract 2.449489742783178 = -0.3066251611932115

So right side = -0.3066251611932115 / 2 = -0.15331258059660575.

Thus, y = (-0.15331258059660575) / (-0.23267301202104) = 0.6588. So y_O ≈ 0.6588. That is not small. So our earlier numeric O_y=0.2286 was likely wrong due to rounding errors. Let's recompute O_y using the coordinates we have. We have B, E, F coordinates. Let's compute O by solving perpendicular bisector of BE and BF. But we already used the vertical line x=X. That should be correct. So O = (X, y_O) with y_O given by that formula. So y_O ≈ 0.6588. That is much larger than the radius? Let's compute radius R = distance from O to B. B = (b_x, b_y) with b_x = √2 - √3/2 ≈ 1.4142135623730951 - 0.8660254037844386 = 0.5481881585886565, b_y = 0.5. X = (3√2 - 2√3)/2 = (3*1.4142135623730951 - 2*1.7320508075688772)/2 = (4.242640687119285 - 3.4641016151377544)/2 = 0.7785390719815306/2 = 0.3892695359907653. So O = (0.38927, 0.6588). Then R^2 = (0.38927-0.54819)^2 + (0.6588-0.5)^2 = (-0.15892)^2 + (0.1588)^2 = 0.02527 + 0.02522 = 0.05049. So R≈0.2246. So radius ≈0.2246.

Now, line L through H=(0.636,0) with direction a=(0.8660,0.5). We need distance from O to L. Let's compute L: param (0.636+0.866t, 0+0.5t). Normal vector n = (0.5, -0.866). Equation: 0.5(x-0.636) -0.866(y-0)=0 => 0.5x -0.318 -0.866y=0 => 0.5x -0.866y =0.318. Now, compute distance from O=(0.38927,0.6588) to this line: |0.5*0.38927 -0.866*0.6588 -0.318| / sqrt(0.5^2+0.866^2) = |0.194635 -0.5705 -0.318| = | -0.6939| =0.6939. That is much larger than radius 0.2246, so the line is far away. So the line through H parallel to AP is not tangent; it's completely outside the circle. So the statement fails in this orientation. But we tested the rotated orientation (where AP is horizontal) and it held. So there is a contradiction: The problem statement is invariant under rotation, so if it holds in one orientation, it must hold in all orientations. Therefore, our test in the rotated orientation must be wrong. Let's re-evaluate the rotated orientation numeric test with higher precision.

In the rotated orientation, we had A=(1,0), C=(cos15°, sin15°), D=(cos(-75°), sin(-75°)). We computed B, M, N, H, O, and found that the horizontal line through H is tangent. Let's recompute that with exact values if possible.

First, recall the rotated orientation came from rotating the original configuration by -30°. In that rotated system, the points are:

A = (1,0)
C = (cos15°, sin15°) = (cos15°, sin15°)
D = (cos(-75°), sin(-75°)) = (cos75°, -sin75°) because cos(-θ)=cosθ, sin(-θ)=-sinθ.

Thus, C = (cos15°, sin15°), D = (cos75°, -sin75°). Let's use exact values: cos15° = (√6+√2)/4 ≈ 0.9659258262890683, sin15° = (√6-√2)/4 ≈ 0.25881904510252076. cos75° = sin15° = (√6-√2)/4 ≈ 0.25881904510252076, sin75° = cos15° = (√6+√2)/4 ≈ 0.9659258262890683. So D = ( (√6-√2)/4, -(√6+√2)/4 ). So coordinates:

A = (1,0)
C = (c_x, c_y) = ((√6+√2)/4, (√6-√2)/4)
D = (d_x, d_y) = ((√6-√2)/4, -(√6+√2)/4)

Now, compute M: intersection of line CD with perpendicular bisector of AC.

First, find line CD. Compute vector v = D - C = ( (√6-√2)/4 - (√6+√2)/4, -(√6+√2)/4 - (√6-√2)/4 ) = ( (√6-√2-√6-√2)/4, ( -√6-√2-√6+√2)/4 ) = ( (-2√2)/4, (-2√6)/4 ) = ( -√2/2, -√6/2 ). So v = (-√2/2, -√6/2). Its length = sqrt( (2/4) + (6/4) ) = sqrt(8/4)= sqrt(2)=1.4142. So unit direction u = v/|v| = (-√2/2, -√6/2)/√2 = (-1/2, -√3/2)? Because √2/2 divided by √2 = 1/2, and √6/2 divided by √2 = √3/2. So u = (-1/2, -√3/2). So line CD: param C + t v.

Now, compute perpendicular bisector of AC. A=(1,0), C=(c_x,c_y). Midpoint of AC: ((1+c_x)/2, c_y/2). Direction of AC: (c_x-1, c_y). A perpendicular vector: (c_y, 1-c_x). So perpendicular bisector: (x,y) = midpoint + s (c_y, 1-c_x).

Now, we need intersection with line CD. Solve C + t v = midpoint + s (c_y, 1-c_x).

Let’s compute all exact.

c_x = (√6+√2)/4, c_y = (√6-√2)/4.

Compute 1-c_x = 1 - (√6+√2)/4 = (4 - √6 - √2)/4.
c_y = (√6-√2)/4.

So the perpendicular bisector: (x,y) = ( (1+c_x)/2, c_y/2 ) + s (c_y, 1-c_x). So:

x = (1+c_x)/2 + s c_y
y = c_y/2 + s (1-c_x)

Now, line CD: (x,y) = C + t v = (c_x - t √2/2, c_y - t √6/2). (Since v = (-√2/2, -√6/2)).

Set equal:

c_x - t √2/2 = (1+c_x)/2 + s c_y   ...(1)
c_y - t √6/2 = c_y/2 + s (1-c_x)   ...(2)

We can solve for t,s. From (2): c_y - t √6/2 = c_y/2 + s (1-c_x) => c_y/2 - t √6/2 = s (1-c_x) => s = (c_y/2 - t √6/2) / (1-c_x). Provided 1-c_x ≠ 0.

From (1): c_x - t √2/2 = (1+c_x)/2 + s c_y => substitute s.

Let’s compute 1-c_x = (4-√6-√2)/4. c_y = (√6-√2)/4. So c_y/2 = (√6-√2)/8. √6/2 = √6/2. So (c_y/2 - t √6/2) = (√6-√2)/8 - t √6/2 = (√6-√2 - 4t√6)/8 = (√6(1-4t) - √2)/8.

Thus, s = [ (√6(1-4t) - √2)/8 ] / ( (4-√6-√2)/4 ) = [ (√6(1-4t) - √2)/8 ] * [4/(4-√6-√2)] = [ (√6(1-4t) - √2) ] / [2(4-√6-√2)].

Now, plug into (1):

c_x - t √2/2 = (1+c_x)/2 + s c_y.

Compute left: c_x - t √2/2 = (√6+√2)/4 - t √2/2 = (√6+√2)/4 - (2t√2)/4 = (√6+√2 - 2t√2)/4.

Right: (1+c_x)/2 = (1 + (√6+√2)/4)/2 = ( (4+√6+√2)/4 )/2 = (4+√6+√2)/8.
s c_y = [ (√6(1-4t) - √2) / (2(4-√6-√2)) ] * ( (√6-√2)/4 ) = [ (√6(1-4t) - √2)(√6-√2) ] / [8(4-√6-√2)].

Thus, equation: (√6+√2 - 2t√2)/4 = (4+√6+√2)/8 + [ (√6(1-4t) - √2)(√6-√2) ] / [8(4-√6-√2)].

Multiply both sides by 8: 2(√6+√2 - 2t√2) = (4+√6+√2) + [ (√6(1-4t) - √2)(√6-√2) ] / (4-√6-√2).

So 2√6+2√2 - 4t√2 = 4+√6+√2 + [ (√6(1-4t) - √2)(√6-√2) ] / (4-√6-√2).

Bring terms: (2√6+2√2 - 4t√2) - (4+√6+√2) = [ (√6(1-4t) - √2)(√6-√2) ] / (4-√6-√2). Left: (2√6+2√2 - √6 - √2 -4) -4t√2 = (√6+√2-4) -4t√2. So:

(√6+√2-4) -4t√2 = [ (√6(1-4t) - √2)(√6-√2) ] / (4-√6-√2).

Now, note that (√6-√2) = (√6-√2). Also, (4-√6-√2) = (4-√6-√2). Multiply both sides by (4-√6-√2):

(√6+√2-4)(4-√6-√2) -4t√2 (4-√6-√2) = (√6(1-4t) - √2)(√6-√2).

Compute (√6+√2-4)(4-√6-√2) = -(4-√6-√2)(4-√6-√2)? Actually, (√6+√2-4) = -(4-√6-√2). So product = -(4-√6-√2)^2. So left side = -(4-√6-√2)^2 -4t√2 (4-√6-√2). So equation:

-(4-√6-√2)^2 -4t√2 (4-√6-√2) = (√6(1-4t) - √2)(√6-√2).

Now, compute (4-√6-√2)^2 = 16 + 6 + 2 -8√6 -8√2 +2√12 = 24 -8√6 -8√2 +4√3? Since 2√12=4√3. So = 24 -8√6 -8√2 +4√3.

Thus, left side = - (24 -8√6 -8√2 +4√3) -4t√2 (4-√6-√2) = -24 +8√6 +8√2 -4√3 -4t√2 (4-√6-√2).

Now, compute right side: (√6(1-4t) - √2)(√6-√2) = (√6 -4t√6 - √2)(√6-√2). Expand: (√6 - √2 -4t√6)(√6-√2) = (√6-√2)^2 -4t√6(√6-√2). (√6-√2)^2 = 6+2-2√12 = 8-4√3. And √6(√6-√2)=6-√12=6-2√3. So right side = (8-4√3) -4t(6-2√3) = 8-4√3 -24t +8t√3.

Thus, equation:

-24 +8√6 +8√2 -4√3 -4t√2 (4-√6-√2) = 8-4√3 -24t +8t√3.

Bring terms: -24 +8√6 +8√2 -4√3 -4t√2 (4-√6-√2) -8 +4√3 +24t -8t√3 =0 => -32 +8√6 +8√2 -4√3 +4√3? Actually, -4√3+4√3=0. So left = -32 +8√6 +8√2 -4t√2 (4-√6-√2) +24t -8t√3 =0.

Thus, -32 +8√6 +8√2 + t(24 -4√2(4-√6-√2) -8√3) =0.

We can use the formula for the circumcenter in barycentric coordinates. For triangle with points (x1,y1), (x2,y2), (x3,y3), the circumcenter coordinates (X,Y) satisfy:

| X - x1 |^2 = | X - x2 |^2 = | X - x3 |^2.

This gives two linear equations. Let's solve.

Set up equations:

(1) |O - B|^2 = |O - E|^2 => (X - b_x)^2 + (Y - b_y)^2 = (X - e_x)^2 + (Y - e_y)^2.
(2) |O - B|^2 = |O - F|^2 => (X - b_x)^2 + (Y - b_y)^2 = (X - f_x)^2 + (Y - f_y)^2.

Expand (1): X^2 -2X b_x + b_x^2 + Y^2 -2Y b_y + b_y^2 = X^2 -2X e_x + e_x^2 + Y^2 -2Y e_y + e_y^2 => cancel X^2,Y^2: -2X b_x -2Y b_y + b_x^2+b_y^2 = -2X e_x -2Y e_y + e_x^2+e_y^2 => 2X(e_x - b_x) + 2Y(e_y - b_y) = e_x^2+e_y^2 - b_x^2 - b_y^2. (Eq1)

Similarly, (2): 2X(f_x - b_x) + 2Y(f_y - b_y) = f_x^2+f_y^2 - b_x^2 - b_y^2. (Eq2)

N = (cosθ, y_N) with y_N = - (sinδ cosθ)/cosδ.
B = (2cosθ - cosα, sinα)
E = t_E A, F = t_F A, where t_E = 2 cosθ cosδ / cosσ - 1, t_F = 2 cosθ cosσ / cosδ - 1.
H = (cos^2θ/(cosσ cosδ), 0).

Now, we want O = (X,Y) such that |O-B|^2 = |O-E|^2 and |O-B|^2 = |O-F|^2, and also O lies on perpendicular bisector of EF? Actually, that is automatically satisfied if we use the equidistance conditions; but we need to find O. We can use the two linear equations:

2X(e_x - b_x) + 2Y(e_y - b_y) = e_x^2+e_y^2 - b_x^2 - b_y^2.   (Eq1)
2X(f_x - b_x) + 2Y(f_y - b_y) = f_x^2+f_y^2 - b_x^2 - b_y^2.   (Eq2)

But we can also use the fact that O lies on the perpendicular bisector of EF, which is the line through the midpoint of EF perpendicular to OA. The midpoint of EF is μ A with μ = (t_E+t_F)/2. The perpendicular direction is A_perp. So the equation is (X,Y) dot A = μ. That is a linear equation.

Thus, we have three linear equations, but only two unknowns; they are consistent. So we can solve for X,Y.

Let’s define vectors in the plane. We can use the basis (A, A_perp) as before. Let’s set:

A = (cosα, sinα)
A_perp = (-sinα, cosα)

Then any point X = u A + v A_perp, with coordinates (u,v). Then:

- A = (1,0)
- B = (b_u, b_v) where b_u = A·B = 2 cosα cosθ - cos2α? Actually, A·B = cosα(2cosθ - cosα) + sinα sinα = 2 cosα cosθ - cos^2α + sin^2α = 2 cosα cosθ - cos2α. And b_v = A_perp·B = -sinα(2cosθ - cosα) + cosα sinα = -2 sinα cosθ + sinα cosα + cosα sinα = -2 sinα cosθ + 2 sinα cosα = 2 sinα (cosα - cosθ).

- E = (t_E, 0)
- F = (t_F, 0)
- μ = (t_E+t_F)/2
- H = (h_u, h_v) with h_u = A·H = cosα * x_H + sinα * 0 = x_H cosα, h_v = A_perp·H = -sinα * x_H + cosα * 0 = -x_H sinα. So h_u = x_H cosα, h_v = -x_H sinα.

Now, O = (u_O, v_O) with u_O = μ (since O lies on perpendicular bisector of EF: O·A = μ). So u_O = μ. Then O = (μ, v_O). Now, we need to find v_O from the condition that |O-B|^2 = |O-E|^2. In (u,v) coordinates, this is:

(μ - b_u)^2 + (v_O - b_v)^2 = (μ - t_E)^2 + (v_O - 0)^2.

Expand: μ^2 -2μ b_u + b_u^2 + v_O^2 -2v_O b_v + b_v^2 = μ^2 -2μ t_E + t_E^2 + v_O^2.

Cancel μ^2, v_O^2: -2μ b_u + b_u^2 -2v_O b_v + b_v^2 = -2μ t_E + t_E^2.

Thus, -2v_O b_v = -2μ t_E + t_E^2 +2μ b_u - b_u^2 - b_v^2.

So v_O = ( -2μ t_E + t_E^2 +2μ b_u - b_u^2 - b_v^2 ) / ( -2 b_v ) = ( 2μ (t_E - b_u) + t_E^2 - b_u^2 - b_v^2 ) / (2 b_v). Actually, careful: -2v_O b_v = -2μ t_E + t_E^2 +2μ b_u - b_u^2 - b_v^2 => multiply by -1: 2v_O b_v = 2μ t_E - t_E^2 -2μ b_u + b_u^2 + b_v^2 => v_O = ( 2μ (t_E - b_u) + b_u^2 + b_v^2 - t_E^2 ) / (2 b_v). So:

v_O = ( 2μ (t_E - b_u) + (b_u^2 + b_v^2 - t_E^2) ) / (2 b_v).

Similarly, from |O-B|^2 = |O-F|^2, we would get the same expression with t_F, and consistency requires that (t_F^2 - t_E^2) = 2μ(t_F - t_E) which holds because μ = (t_E+t_F)/2. So the formula is valid.

Now, we also need to compute R = distance from O to B: R^2 = (μ - b_u)^2 + (v_O - b_v)^2.

The line L is through H parallel to A. In (u,v) coordinates, A is x-axis, so line L is horizontal line through H: v = h_v. So distance from O to L is |v_O - h_v|.

We need to show that |v_O - h_v| = R.

Thus, we need to verify:

(v_O - h_v)^2 = (μ - b_u)^2 + (v_O - b_v)^2.

Expand: v_O^2 -2v_O h_v + h_v^2 = μ^2 -2μ b_u + b_u^2 + v_O^2 -2v_O b_v + b_v^2.

Cancel v_O^2: -2v_O h_v + h_v^2 = μ^2 -2μ b_u + b_u^2 -2v_O b_v + b_v^2.

Bring terms: -2v_O h_v + 2v_O b_v = μ^2 -2μ b_u + b_u^2 + b_v^2 - h_v^2.

So 2v_O (b_v - h_v) = μ^2 -2μ b_u + (b_u^2+b_v^2) - h_v^2.

Thus, v_O = ( μ^2 -2μ b_u + (b_u^2+b_v^2) - h_v^2 ) / (2 (b_v - h_v)).

Now, we have two expressions for v_O. They must be equal, and this equality is the identity we need to prove. This is a purely algebraic identity in the variables α,θ (with σ,δ defined). We can attempt to simplify using the expressions for these variables.

Place the configuration so that P is the origin and the circumcircle of triangle ACD is the unit circle. After a rotation, we can assume that the line MN (which contains C and D) is vertical. Because the circles Ω and Γ intersect at A and B, the common chord AB is perpendicular to MN, so AB is horizontal. The reflection of A across the line x=k gives B = (2k - cosα, sinα). The centers M and N lie on the line x=k, and M is the circumcenter of triangle ABC, N of ABD. Computing the perpendicular bisector of AC gives M = (k, y_M) with y_M = (sinσ cosθ)/cosσ, where σ = (α+θ)/2, δ = (θ-α)/2, and θ = arccos(k). Similarly, N = (k, y_N) with y_N = - (sinδ cosθ)/cosδ.

The line AP passes through the origin and A, so its direction is A. The second intersections of AP with circles Ω and Γ are E = t_E A and F = t_F A, where t_E = 2(A·M)-1 and t_F = 2(A·N)-1. Using the identities A·M = cosθ cosδ / cosσ and A·N = cosθ cosσ / cosδ, we obtain t_E = 2 cosθ cosδ / cosσ - 1, t_F = 2 cosθ cosσ / cosδ - 1.

The orthocenter H of triangle PMN (with P=0) lies on the x-axis because N-M = (0, y_N - y_M). Setting H = (x_H, 0), the condition H·N = M·N gives x_H = (M·N)/cosθ. With M·N = cos^2θ + y_M y_N = cos^3θ / (cosσ cosδ), we find x_H = cos^2θ / (cosσ cosδ). So H = (cos^2θ/(cosσ cosδ), 0).

The line L through H parallel to AP has direction A, so its equation in Cartesian coordinates is sinα (x - x_H) - cosα y = 0, i.e., sinα x - cosα y = sinα x_H. (Equation L)

So we have two equations:

|a - c - α v| = α |v|,   (1)
|a - c - β v| = (1-β) |v|.   (2)

Since |v|>0, we can square and divide.

Let’s denote w = a - c. Then (1): |w - α v| = α |v|. Square: |w|^2 - 2α Re(w \overline{v}) + α^2 |v|^2 = α^2 |v|^2 => |w|^2 - 2α Re(w \overline{v}) = 0 => α = |w|^2 / (2 Re(w \overline{v})). Provided Re(w \overline{v}) > 0. Since α>0, we need Re(w \overline{v})>0.

Similarly, (2): |w - β v| = (1-β) |v|. Square: |w|^2 - 2β Re(w \overline{v}) + β^2 |v|^2 = (1-β)^2 |v|^2 = (1 - 2β + β^2) |v|^2 => |w|^2 - 2β Re(w \overline{v}) + β^2 |v|^2 = |v|^2 - 2β |v|^2 + β^2 |v|^2 => |w|^2 - 2β Re(w \overline{v}) = |v|^2 - 2β |v|^2 => |w|^2 - 2β Re(w \overline{v}) = |v|^2 (1 - 2β) => bring terms: |w|^2 - 2β Re(w \overline{v}) - |v|^2 + 2β |v|^2 = 0 => (|w|^2 - |v|^2) - 2β (Re(w \overline{v}) - |v|^2) = 0 => β = (|w|^2 - |v|^2) / (2 (Re(w \overline{v}) - |v|^2)). Maybe there is a simpler way: Since we are only interested in tangency condition, perhaps we can show that the line through H parallel to OA is the polar of A with respect to circle (BEF) or something. Or maybe we can prove that H is the orthocenter of triangle P? Not.

Given the complexity of the algebra, perhaps we can find a synthetic proof.

Let's reconsider the problem with a synthetic approach.

Given circles Ω (center M, radius r) and Γ (center N, radius R), with r<R, intersecting at A and B. Line MN meets Ω at C (on the side of M opposite N) and Γ at D (on the side of N opposite M). So M is between C and N, N between M and D. So C, M, N, D collinear in that order.

Define P = circumcenter of triangle ACD. So P is the intersection of perpendicular bisectors of AC, AD, and CD. Since M is the circumcenter of ABC, we have M lies on the perpendicular bisector of AC. Similarly, N lies on the perpendicular bisector of AD. And P lies on both perpendicular bisectors of AC and AD. Therefore, PM is the perpendicular bisector of AC? Actually, P and M both lie on the perpendicular bisector of AC, so line PM is the perpendicular bisector of AC. Similarly, line PN is the perpendicular bisector of AD. So PM ⟂ AC and PN ⟂ AD.

Thus, triangle PMN has the property that the lines PM and PN are perpendicular to AC and AD respectively.

Now, consider the circumcircle of triangle ACD with center P. Points A, C, D lie on this circle.

We have AP meets Ω again at E and Γ again at F. So E and F are on Ω and Γ respectively, and also on line AP.

We also have H is orthocenter of triangle PMN. So H is the intersection of the altitudes from P, M, N.

Since M and N lie on line MN, the altitude from P is the line through P perpendicular to MN. The altitude from M is the line through M perpendicular to PN. The altitude from N is the line through N perpendicular to PM.

Now, note that PM ⟂ AC and PN ⟂ AD. So the altitude from M is perpendicular to PN, but PN is perpendicular to AD, so the altitude from M is parallel to AD? Because if line ℓ is perpendicular to PN, and PN ⟂ AD, then ℓ ∥ AD. Indeed, two lines perpendicular to the same line are parallel. So the altitude from M (through M) is perpendicular to PN, and since PN ⟂ AD, we have altitude from M ∥ AD. Similarly, altitude from N (through N) is perpendicular to PM, and since PM ⟂ AC, we have altitude from N ∥ AC. And altitude from P is perpendicular to MN.

Thus, we have:

- Line through M parallel to AD (since altitude from M ∥ AD) is the altitude from M, so it passes through H.
- Line through N parallel to AC (altitude from N) is the altitude from N, so it passes through H.
- Line through P perpendicular to MN is the altitude from P, so it passes through H.

So H is the intersection of:
- line through M parallel to AD,
- line through N parallel to AC,
- line through P perpendicular to MN.

Thus, we have a nice description: H is the point such that MH ∥ AD, NH ∥ AC, and PH ⟂ MN. (Check: Since altitude from M is perpendicular to PN, and PN ⟂ AD, so altitude from M ∥ AD. Yes.)

Now, we want to prove that the line through H parallel to AP is tangent to circumcircle of triangle BEF.

Since AP is a line, the line through H parallel to AP is just a line in the direction of AP. So L = line through H with direction AP.

Now, we need to relate this to circle (BEF). Maybe we can show that L is the Simson line of H with respect to triangle BEF? But H is not necessarily on the circumcircle. However, there is a concept: The line through H parallel to AP might be the tangent at the point where the circle (BEF) meets the line through H that is the reflection of something.

Alternatively, maybe we can show that the circle (BEF) is the image of the circle (ACD) under some homothety or inversion, and that L is tangent to it because it is the image of a tangent to the original circle.

Since P is circumcenter of ACD, and AP is a line through A and P. Consider the circle (ACD). Its tangent at A is perpendicular to PA. But we have AP line, not tangent. However, note that the line through H parallel to AP is parallel to AP. Could it be that H lies on the polar of A with respect to circle (ACD)? Let's check: For a circle with center P, the polar of point A is the line through the point that is the inverse of A? Actually, if P is center, then the polar of a point X (not at center) is the line perpendicular to PX through the inversion of X? Not.

But maybe we can use coordinate geometry now with the simplified coordinate system: unit circle centered at P=0, C and D symmetric about real axis, A = e^{iα}. Then we can compute all points in terms of α and θ. Then the condition that line through H parallel to OA (i.e., direction a) is tangent to circle (BEF) can be computed algebraically. This is a good plan: We have explicit formulas for points in terms of α and θ. We can compute the circle (BEF) and verify that the distance from its center to line L equals its radius. Since we are in a coordinate system, we can use algebra with trigonometric simplifications. The result should be independent of α and θ (subject to intersection condition). So we can proceed with analytic geometry.

Set P = (0,0) origin. Let unit circle: x^2+y^2=1. Let c = (cosθ, sinθ), d = (cosθ, -sinθ). Let a = (cosα, sinα). Assume α and θ such that circles Ω and Γ intersect. We need to ensure that A is on both circles, which will be satisfied by our definitions of M and N as intersections of line x=cosθ with perpendicular bisectors of AC and AD. But we also need that the circles Ω and Γ exist with centers M and N on line x=cosθ, and they pass through A and B (the reflection of A across line x=cosθ). The geometry imposes conditions on α and θ: Since M is the intersection of x=cosθ with perpendicular bisector of AC, and N similarly for AD, and the circles must intersect at A and B. We already have that B is the reflection of A across line x=cosθ, so B = (2cosθ - cosα, sinα). For the circles Ω and Γ to intersect at A and B, we need that M is equidistant from A and B (since both lie on Ω). That is true if M lies on the perpendicular bisector of AB. Since line MN is vertical line x=cosθ, and AB is horizontal line y=sinα, the perpendicular bisector of AB is the vertical line through the midpoint of AB. The midpoint of AB is ((cosα+2cosθ-cosα)/2, (sinα+sinα)/2) = (cosθ, sinα). So the perpendicular bisector of AB is the vertical line x=cosθ. So indeed, any point on x=cosθ is equidistant from A and B. So M and N are on that line, so they are equidistant from A and B. That is consistent. Since a is unit, this is a linear equation.

The circumcenter O is the intersection of this perpendicular bisector with the perpendicular bisector of BE (or BF). So O satisfies a·O = (e+f)/2. Also, O is equidistant from B and E: |O - B|^2 = |O - E|^2. That gives another equation. We can solve for O.

Set:

P = (0,0)
A = (1,0)

Let’s compute coordinates.

Let’s denote:

c = (c_x, c_y) = (cosφ, sinφ)
d = (d_x, d_y) = (cosψ, sinψ)

Line CD param: X = c + t(d-c) = (c_x + t(d_x-c_x), c_y + t(d_y-c_y))

Now, perpendicular bisector of AC:

A=(1,0), C=(c_x,c_y). Midpoint: ((1+c_x)/2, c_y/2). Direction of AC: (c_x-1, c_y). A perpendicular vector: (c_y, 1-c_x) (since dot: (c_x-1)*c_y + c_y*(1-c_x)=0). So the line: Y = ((1+c_x)/2, c_y/2) + s (c_y, 1-c_x).

Set c + t(d-c) = ((1+c_x)/2, c_y/2) + s (c_y, 1-c_x).

This gives two equations:

c_x + t(d_x-c_x) = (1+c_x)/2 + s c_y   ...(1)
c_y + t(d_y-c_y) = c_y/2 + s (1-c_x)   ...(2)

We can solve for t and s.

From (2): c_y + t(d_y-c_y) = c_y/2 + s(1-c_x) => (c_y/2) + t(d_y-c_y) = s(1-c_x) => s = (c_y/2 + t(d_y-c_y))/(1-c_x), provided 1-c_x ≠ 0 (i.e., φ not 0). If φ=0, then C=A, degenerate. So we assume φ≠0, and similarly ψ≠0.

From (1): c_x + t(d_x-c_x) = (1+c_x)/2 + s c_y => substitute s.

Let’s compute:

c_x + t(d_x-c_x) = (1+c_x)/2 + c_y * (c_y/2 + t(d_y-c_y))/(1-c_x)

Multiply both sides by (1-c_x):

(1-c_x)(c_x + t(d_x-c_x)) = (1-c_x)(1+c_x)/2 + c_y (c_y/2 + t(d_y-c_y))

Compute left: (1-c_x)c_x + t(1-c_x)(d_x-c_x) = c_x - c_x^2 + t(1-c_x)(d_x-c_x)

Right: (1-c_x)(1+c_x)/2 = (1-c_x^2)/2 + c_y^2/2 + t c_y (d_y-c_y)

Thus, we have:

c_x - c_x^2 + t(1-c_x)(d_x-c_x) = (1-c_x^2)/2 + c_y^2/2 + t c_y (d_y-c_y)

But c_x^2 + c_y^2 = 1. So 1-c_x^2 = c_y^2. So (1-c_x^2)/2 = c_y^2/2. Then the right side becomes c_y^2/2 + c_y^2/2 + t c_y (d_y-c_y) = c_y^2 + t c_y (d_y-c_y). So the equation simplifies to:

c_x - c_x^2 + t(1-c_x)(d_x-c_x) = c_y^2 + t c_y (d_y-c_y)

But c_x - c_x^2 = c_x(1-c_x). And c_y^2 = 1 - c_x^2 = (1-c_x)(1+c_x). So c_x(1-c_x) + t(1-c_x)(d_x-c_x) = (1-c_x)(1+c_x) + t c_y (d_y-c_y). Factor (1-c_x) from left: (1-c_x)[c_x + t(d_x-c_x)] = (1-c_x)(1+c_x) + t c_y (d_y-c_y). Since 1-c_x ≠ 0, divide both sides by (1-c_x):

c_x + t(d_x-c_x) = 1+c_x + t c_y (d_y-c_y)/(1-c_x). So:

t(d_x-c_x) = 1 + t c_y (d_y-c_y)/(1-c_x). So:

t(d_x-c_x) - t c_y (d_y-c_y)/(1-c_x) = 1 => t[ (d_x-c_x) - c_y (d_y-c_y)/(1-c_x) ] = 1. So t = 1 / [ (d_x-c_x) - c_y (d_y-c_y)/(1-c_x) ].
